Section Three

# Culture, Customs & Daily Life

Culture is the texture of your world — the unwritten rules everyone knows, the rituals that mark important moments, the things people do without thinking. It's how people greet each other, what they eat, what they believe about luck, how they raise their children, and what they do for fun.

✦

*This section is divided into two parts:*

**Part One: The Foundation** — How people actually live. Daily routines, family structures, religion, manners, entertainment, food. This is the lived-in world your characters inhabit regardless of whether they're falling in love.

**Part Two: Culture & Romance** — How cultural rules create romantic tension. What's forbidden, what's expected, how courtship works, what happens when rules are broken. This builds on the foundation to show how your world's culture specifically affects your love story.

You need both.

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question will apply to your story. **Skip what doesn't fit.**

**If your world mirrors a real culture, focus on what's different.** You don't need to invent every custom from scratch. Focus on what deviates from what readers will assume, and on elements that create friction for your romance.

## How Culture Works in Romantasy

Culture in romantasy functions as a **constraint system** — the social rules that determine what your characters can and can't do, what costs they pay for transgression, and what hoops they must jump through to be together.

The tighter the constraints, the higher the tension. A culture where unmarried people can't be alone together makes every stolen moment electric. Loose constraints can work too — but then your tension needs to come from somewhere else.

> #### Culture as Obstacle
>
> The rules your characters must navigate or break:
>
> - Taboos against certain relationships (class, species, enemy nations)
> - Required courtship progressions that can't be skipped
> - Chaperoning requirements that prevent privacy
> - Reputation systems where being seen together causes damage
> - Family involvement that creates external opposition

> #### Culture as Opportunity
>
> The customs that create romantic moments:
>
> - Festivals where normal rules relax
> - Dances that allow sanctioned touch
> - Gift-giving traditions that let characters express interest
> - Ceremonies that force proximity
> - Traditions that create meaningful gestures

> #### Culture as Revelation
>
> How characters interact with cultural expectations shows who they are:
>
> - Do they follow rules out of belief or fear?
> - Do they break rules impulsively or deliberately?
> - Do they chafe against expectations or never question them?
>
> The most interesting romantic tension often comes from pairing characters who relate to their culture differently — one who embodies its values, one who challenges them.

## Common Patterns: Culture in Romantasy

### How Cultural Rules Create Romantic Tension

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The forbidden touch** | Culture restricts physical contact; every brush of hands becomes charged | Slow-burn tension; meaningful small gestures |
| **The reputation gamble** | Being seen together risks social ruin | Stakes for every private moment; secrets |
| **The courtship ladder** | Expected progression that can't be skipped | Frustration when feelings outpace propriety |
| **The chaperoned dance** | Must interact under watchful eyes | Stolen glances; coded communication |
| **The festival exception** | One night when rules relax | Pivotal scenes; masks and anonymity |
| **The cultural mistranslation** | Gesture means different things in different cultures | Misunderstanding; revelation when explained |

## Common Mistakes with Cultural Worldbuilding

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #1: The Inconsistent Culture
>
> **What it looks like:** Cultural rules apply when convenient and disappear when inconvenient. Characters are chaperoned in one scene and alone together the next with no explanation.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Stakes that come and go feel fake. If readers can't trust that the rules are real, they can't feel tension about breaking them.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Establish your rules clearly and enforce them consistently. If characters break rules, show consequences. If rules relax in certain circumstances, establish that pattern early.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #2: The Paper-Thin Taboo
>
> **What it looks like:** Something is supposedly forbidden, but no one seems to actually care. The "forbidden" lovers face disapproval but no real consequences.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Forbidden love only matters if the forbidding is real. Stakes need teeth.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Make consequences concrete and serious. Show other characters who suffered these consequences so readers know the threat is real.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #3: The Monolithic Culture
>
> **What it looks like:** Everyone in a society thinks the same way, follows the same rules. No internal variation, no rebels, no regional differences.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Show variation. Some people enforce rules strictly; others look the other way. Older and younger generations may differ. Your characters can find allies and enemies within their own culture.

Section 3 · Part One

# The Foundation

## Daily Life & Routines

Before you can understand how culture affects romance, you need to understand how people actually live. Romance happens *within* daily life. Understanding these patterns tells you when characters might cross paths and what "normal" looks like.

**How to write it:** You don't need to show every routine — just enough to establish a baseline so readers feel the disruption when it breaks. A few sentences of morning ritual (the tea always taken, the prayer always said) make it meaningful when your character is too distracted to complete them. Use routine to reveal character: what they do first, what they skip when rushed, what small comfort they never sacrifice. When romance intrudes on routine — when they start waking thinking of someone, when the familiar walk home feels charged because they might see that person — readers feel the shift because you've established what normal looked like.

#### What's the rhythm of a typical day?

*When might your protagonist naturally cross paths with their love interest? What parts of the day offer opportunity for private moments?*

| Time | What Happens |
| --- | --- |
| Morning | Prayer at dawn; light breakfast; nobles receive petitioners while commoners begin work in fields or shops |
| Morning |  |
| Midday |  |
| Afternoon/Evening |  |
| Night |  |

*Market days? Rest days? Religious observances? Regular social gatherings?*

[Your answer here]

## Home & Household

**How to write it:** Describe homes through what the POV character notices — and what they don't notice reveals their own background. A character raised in poverty catalogues every expensive object; one raised wealthy might not register luxury at all but notice immediately when something is "wrong." Use specific objects rather than general descriptions: not "a wealthy home" but "silver candlesticks left casually on a side table, more books than she'd seen in her life, a fire burning in an empty room." Let the home reveal character history through accumulated objects, wear patterns, what's been mended versus replaced. When a character enters their love interest's home for the first time, slow down — this is them understanding who this person is through where they live.

#### What does a typical home look like for different classes?

*What would your protagonist notice entering the love interest's home for the first time? What reveals their background or values?*

| Class | Home Description |
| --- | --- |
| Wealthy/noble |  |
| Middle/merchant |  |
| Common/poor |  |

## Food & Meals

Food reveals culture. What people eat, how they prepare it, and who they share it with tells you everything about values, resources, and social bonds.

You don't need to invent an entire cuisine. Focus on the details that matter for your story: What would your characters share over dinner? What meal might they cook for someone they love?

#### What's the staple food in your world?

*Bread cultures feel different from rice cultures, which feel different from potato or maize cultures. What fills bellies?*

[Your answer here]

#### What's a typical meal for different classes?

*The gap between what nobles eat and what commoners eat reveals social divides your characters might cross.*

| Class | Typical Meal |
| --- | --- |
| Wealthy/noble | Multiple courses, meat daily, imported spices, wine — meals are social performances |
| Common/poor | Bread, pottage, vegetables in season, meat only on feast days — meals are fuel |

| Common/poor |  |

## Architecture & Building

How people build says a lot about who they are. A desert palace won't look anything like a northern fortress. You don't need to be an architect to figure this out.

Just think about what makes sense. Hot climates need shade and airflow. Cold climates need thick walls and big hearths. When your characters travel between regions, these differences help readers feel the change.

#### How Climate Shapes Architecture

*Consider how your world's climate affects how people build.*

| Climate | Architectural Adaptations |
| --- | --- |
| Hot/sunny | Thick walls, small windows, courtyards, flat roofs, light colors, shaded walkways, cross-ventilation |
| Rainy/humid | Steep roofs, covered walkways, raised foundations, gutter systems, overhangs, indoor-focused living |
| Cold/snowy | Thick insulation, steep roofs for snow, small windows, central hearths, vestibules/mudrooms |
| Windy/coastal | Low profiles, reinforced structures, windbreaks, secure shutters, anchored roofs |

#### What architectural style dominates your main setting?

*Research real-world architecture that inspires your world — note distinctive elements like columns, arches, domes, materials.*

[Your answer here]

## Superstitions & Folk Beliefs

Beyond formal religion, most cultures have folk beliefs — things everyone "knows" even if they can't explain why. These small details make a world feel lived-in.

### Reference: Common Superstitions & Omens

*Use this list for inspiration. Pick a few that fit your world's mood and culture.*

| Category | Examples |
| --- | --- |
| **Lucky/Unlucky Numbers** | Three for luck, seven for magic, thirteen cursed, odd numbers for the living, even for the dead |
| **Animal Omens** | Ravens mean death, white animals are blessed, black cats bring luck (or doom), owls warn of secrets |
| **Weather Signs** | Red sky warnings, thunder during weddings, rain on a journey's start, fog hiding spirits |
| **Protective Actions** | Iron wards off fae, salt purifies, crossing thresholds with right foot, spitting to avert evil eye |
| **Bad Luck Triggers** | Speaking a dead person's name, whistling at night, opening umbrellas indoors, breaking mirrors |
| **Good Luck Rituals** | Knocking wood, throwing coins in water, carrying charms, first-footing at new year, bride's "something old" |
| **Threshold Beliefs** | Don't step on cracks, carry brides over doorways, leave offerings at crossroads, dawn/dusk are dangerous |
| **Dreams & Visions** | Dreams of water mean change, falling dreams warn of pride, dreaming of someone means they're thinking of you |

#### What common superstitions exist?

*Lucky/unlucky numbers, colors, animals? Actions that invite bad fortune? Protective gestures?*

[Your answer here]

#### What everyday rituals do people perform without thinking?

*Knocking on wood, crossing fingers, small offerings, phrases spoken at certain moments:*

[Your answer here]

## Manners, Etiquette & Everyday Politeness

**How to write it:** Don't explain the rules — show the violation and its aftermath. When your character commits a social error, write the immediate physical reaction of others: the sharp intake of breath, the exchanged glance, the beat of silence before conversation resumes. Let your character feel the wrongness before they understand it. For deliberate rule-breaking, show the internal calculation: the character knowing what they're risking and choosing to act anyway. Manners become romantic tension when one character watches another navigate their world — the competence or incompetence, the subtle corrections, the moment when the outsider finally gets it right. Small rudeness is often more powerful than large insult; the cut direct, the wrong form of address, the turned shoulder.

#### How do people greet each other?

*What greeting between your characters might feel charged or meaningful? How might your protagonist stumble with unfamiliar customs?*

| Relationship | Greeting |
| --- | --- |
| Strangers |  |
| Friends |  |
| Superior to inferior (class/age/rank) |  |
| Inferior to superior |  |

#### What's considered rude in everyday interaction?

*What faux pas might an outsider commit? What rudeness could create tension or misunderstanding between your characters?*

[Your answer here]

Section 3 · Part Two

# Culture & Romance

## Core Values & Worldview

### Reference: Cultural Values & Traits

*Use this list when deciding what your culture values most. Pick 3-5 that define your world.*

| Category | Possible Values |
| --- | --- |
| **Personal Virtues** | Honor, loyalty, courage, humility, patience, wisdom, honesty, compassion, self-reliance, cunning |
| **Social Bonds** | Family duty, clan loyalty, hospitality, community harmony, respect for elders, friendship, romantic love |
| **Achievement** | Ambition, excellence, competition, innovation, artistic mastery, scholarly pursuit, martial prowess |
| **Spiritual** | Piety, ritual observance, connection to ancestors, mystical insight, fate acceptance, divine service |
| **Material** | Wealth, status, land ownership, beautiful possessions, comfort, security, legacy |
| **Freedom** | Independence, personal choice, adventure, wanderlust, self-expression, breaking tradition |
| **Order** | Tradition, hierarchy, obedience, propriety, law, duty, sacrifice for the greater good |
| **Power** | Strength, dominance, conquest, political influence, magical ability, control, leadership |

Every culture has foundational beliefs — the virtues people are taught from childhood, the qualities that earn respect, the failures that bring shame. Your characters exist within these value systems, shaped by them even when they rebel.

> *The Key Question
>
> Which cultural values must your characters compromise or sacrifice to be together?*

#### What does your culture value most highly?

*Consider: honor, loyalty, strength, cunning, beauty, magical power, knowledge, piety, family, individual achievement, community, tradition, freedom, duty.*

## Taboos & Forbidden Things

What's forbidden reveals what a culture fears. In romantasy, taboos create the "forbidden love" stakes. Taboos work best when specific and consequential.

#### What actions, topics, or behaviors are taboo in your culture?

*Taboos reveal what a society fears. Which taboos will your characters dance around, and which will they ultimately cross?*

| Category | Taboo Behaviors |
| --- | --- |
| Physical (touch, proximity, eye contact) | Unmarried people can't be alone together; touching someone's hair is deeply intimate; sustained eye contact between strangers is aggressive |
| Physical (touch, proximity, eye contact) |  |
| Verbal (certain words, discussing feelings) |  |
| Social (cross-class interaction, being alone together) |  |
| Magical (certain powers, magical contact) |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

## Reputation, Gossip & Public Opinion

In many societies, what people *believe* about you matters as much as what you actually do. Gossip spreads. Reputation is fragile. Once lost, it may never be recovered.

#### What destroys a reputation?

## Touch, Proximity & Physical Boundaries

In many cultures, who can touch whom — and how — is heavily regulated. Touch restrictions are powerful tools for slow-burn romance. When a brush of fingers is transgressive, it carries charge that explicit contact in a permissive culture can't match.

#### What touch is considered appropriate between unmarried people?

*Touch restrictions make every brush of skin meaningful. Define the boundaries your characters will push against.*

| Category | What's Included |
| --- | --- |
| Always acceptable | Formal handshake when introduced; brief touch on arm to get attention; passing objects hand to hand |
| Borderline or scandalous | Hand-holding without gloves; dancing closer than arm's length; any touch lasting more than a moment |
| Always acceptable |  |
| Acceptable in certain contexts |  |
| Borderline or scandalous |  |
| Absolutely forbidden |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### What touch is considered particularly intimate — perhaps more so than a kiss?

*Some cultures consider hand-holding more intimate than other acts. Hair-touching. Using someone's true name. Sustained eye contact. What's YOUR culture's unexpected intimacy?*

[Your answer here]

## Dress, Appearance & Adornment

What people wear signals who they are — class, status, availability, beliefs. Clothing rules create romantic tension: the veil that can't be removed, the color that marks someone as spoken for, the hair that only a spouse may see unbound.

You don't need to describe every stitch. Just know enough to make your descriptions feel true when they matter to the scene.

#### Historical Fashion Terms Reference

If your world draws on medieval or Renaissance styles, here are some common terms. You don't need to use all of them. Pick the ones that feel right for your setting.

*Common terms from medieval/Renaissance fashion, if your world draws on these periods.*

| Term | Description |
| --- | --- |

| Stays/corset | Structured undergarment shaping the torso |
| Petticoat | Underskirt(s) for warmth and volume |
| Jerkin | Sleeveless jacket, often leather |
| Cloak/mantle | Outer wrap for warmth and protection |

## Courtship Customs & Romantic Expectations

Every culture has scripts for romance — expected ways to show interest, accepted progressions from stranger to spouse. Understanding what's "normal" makes deviation meaningful.

**How to write it:** The romantic tension in courtship customs comes from the gap between what's expected and what's felt. A character going through required motions while burning underneath. A character who doesn't know the steps and has to be guided — the intimacy of being taught what to do. Show characters hyper-aware of which step they're on, calculating whether they can skip ahead, frustrated by required pacing that doesn't match their feelings. When characters deviate from the script, make it clear both know what they're doing: "This isn't how it's done," one might say, and the other answers, "I know." The script matters most when it's broken.

#### How do people show romantic interest?

*Consider: gift-giving, specific flowers or tokens, formal requests through family, dances, competitions, magical displays, love letters, poetry, meaningful glances.*

[Your answer here]

#### What's the expected progression of a relationship?

*Map the steps from "strangers" to "married":*

| Step | What Happens |
| --- | --- |
| 1 | Formal introduction through families; suitor sends a white rose to indicate interest |
| 2 | Chaperoned conversations at public events; dancing permitted at balls |
| 1 |  |
| 2 |  |
| 3 |  |
| 4 |  |
| 5 |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### What's the role of family in courtship?

*Must families approve? Arrange meetings? Give formal consent?*

[Your answer here]

## Tokens, Gifts & Symbols

Gift-giving in courtship is a language of its own. The right gift at the right time signals interest; the wrong gift signals presumption or ignorance.

**How to write it:** A gift scene has three beats: the giving, the receiving, and the aftermath. Slow down on each. The giver: what they chose, why, how nervous or confident they are, whether they explain or let the gift speak. The receiver: the moment of recognition (or confusion), the physical handling of the object, the decision of how to respond. The aftermath: where the gift goes, how often it's touched or looked at, whether it's hidden or displayed. The emotional weight of the gift matters more than description of the object — though specific, well-chosen objects carry more meaning than generic "beautiful jewelry." A gift that shows the giver paid attention (remembered a preference, noticed a need) lands harder than an expensive gift that shows only wealth.

#### Are there traditional courtship gifts or tokens?

*What objects carry romantic meaning? (Flowers, jewelry, handkerchiefs, enchanted items, etc.)*

[Your answer here]

#### What does giving or accepting such a token signify?

*Interest? Serious intent? Engagement? Binding commitment?*

[Your answer here]

## Gender Roles & Expectations

Gender — or equivalent social categories in your world — shapes behavior, opportunities, and relationship dynamics. Gender expectations often determine who pursues and who is pursued, who can act and who must wait.

**How to write it:** Show characters bumping against expectations through accumulated small frictions, not just big dramatic confrontations. The dress that restricts movement. The conversation they're excluded from. The assumption about what they want that no one bothers to check. A character who chafes might: take small rebellions (loosened corset, snuck book, hours stolen for practice), modulate behavior based on who's watching, have an internal voice that questions rules they outwardly follow. Show the cost of conforming — the exhaustion, the self-censoring, the moments of private grief for paths not taken. When two characters with different relationships to gender expectations fall for each other, let them see each other differently than the world does.

#### How do these expectations affect your characters?

*Do they conform, resist, or navigate around expectations? How does their relationship to gender roles shape their desires and fears?*

| Character | How Gender Expectations Affect Them |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Does either character violate gender expectations?

*How? What consequences do they face — social, legal, or personal?*

## Children & Education

How a society raises its children reveals what it values most. Some worlds prioritize discipline, others creativity. Some educate everyone equally; others reserve knowledge for the elite.

Think about what childhood looks like in your world. Your characters were shaped by how they were raised — and if they have or will have children, these questions become personal.

#### Who raises children?

*Parents alone? Extended family? Wet nurses and governesses? The entire community? Does it differ by class?*

[Your answer here]

#### How are children educated?

*Formal schooling? Apprenticeships? Home tutoring? Religious instruction? Learning by doing alongside parents?*

| Class/Group | How They're Educated |
| --- | --- |
| Noble children | Private tutors in languages, history, etiquette; boys learn combat and governance; girls learn household management and diplomacy |
| Common children | Learn their parents' trade by working alongside them; basic numeracy from market haggling; most cannot read |
| Noble/wealthy |  |
| Middle/merchant |  |
| Common/poor |  |
| Magical children |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

#### What are children expected to learn?

*Reading and writing? A trade? Religious duties? Combat? Magic? Social graces? Gender-specific skills?*

[Your answer here]

#### At what age do children start working or training?

*When does childhood end in practical terms? When are they expected to contribute?*

[Your answer here]

#### Are there institutions that take children from their families?

*Temples, military academies, magical schools, noble fostering arrangements? What does separation mean?*

[Your answer here]

#### What happens to orphaned children?

*Extended family takes them? Orphanages? The streets? Temple care? Is it a shameful state or simply practical?*

[Your answer here]

#### How were your main characters raised?

*Their upbringing shaped who they are. What was childhood like for them?*

## Festivals, Holidays & Celebrations

Special occasions compress time, force proximity, and create heightened emotional moments. They're classic romance settings — the energy is different, rules may relax, everyone is gathered.

#### What major festivals or celebrations exist in your world?

*Festivals compress emotions and force proximity. Which celebration might serve as a pivotal scene in your romance?*

| Festival | When & What It Celebrates | Key Traditions |
| --- | --- | --- |
| The Veiled Night | Autumn equinox; celebrates the thinning of barriers between worlds | Masquerade ball where identities are hidden; the one night per year when normal courtship rules are suspended — anything that happens "behind the veil" is not acknowledged |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

*Masquerades where identity is hidden? Nights when normal rules are suspended?*

[Your answer here]

## Social Hierarchy in Practice

Even without laws, social hierarchy creates rules. Everyone knows how to behave around their betters and their lessers.

#### What are the unwritten rules of cross-class interaction?

*If your characters come from different social positions, these invisible rules shape every interaction between them.*

| Aspect | Rule |
| --- | --- |
| Eye contact |  |
| Forms of address |  |
| Who speaks first |  |

## Language, Names & Forms of Address

How people speak to each other reveals and creates intimacy. The shift from formal to informal address can be as significant as a first kiss. Names carry power in many fantasy settings.

**How to write it:** Build anticipation for the name-shift by establishing formality early and letting it chafe. Show characters noticing each other's names, wanting to use them differently, catching themselves. The moment of shift needs context: who initiates it, whether it's asked or simply taken, whether the other person reciprocates. Write the physical sensation — the way a name feels different in the mouth, the vulnerability of hearing your first name from someone who's only ever used your title. After the shift, show the newness: the slight stumble, the self-consciousness, the intimacy of being addressed differently than the world addresses you. If one character uses the new name and the other isn't ready, that asymmetry is its own kind of tension.

#### What different forms of address exist?

*The shift from formal to informal address can be as significant as a first kiss. What progression exists in your world?*

| Type | Examples in Your World |
| --- | --- |
| Formal (titles, honorifics, surnames) |  |
| Informal (first names, nicknames) |  |
| Intimate (pet names, private names) |  |

#### When do your characters shift how they address each other?

*This is often a pivotal intimacy moment:*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| From |  |
| To |  |
| When and why |  |

#### Is there significance to names in your world?

*True names with power? Secret names? Multiple names for different contexts?*

[Your answer here]

## Quick Reference: Cultural Stakes

| Element | Your World |
| --- | --- |
| Core cultural value that most affects your romance |  |
| Main taboo your characters break |  |
| The reputation risk |  |
| The touch or proximity rule that creates tension |  |
| The courtship custom they follow or violate |  |
| The festival or ceremony that matters |  |
| The form of address shift (from → to → when) |  |

> *Final Thought
>
> Culture Is Behavior, Not Exposition
>
> Don't info-dump culture. Show it through action: characters following or breaking rules, social awkwardness when norms are violated. The best cultural worldbuilding is invisible until someone breaks the rules — and then everyone knows exactly what line was crossed.*

Section Four

# Economy, Labor & Resources

Money makes the world go round — even fantasy worlds. Economics determines who eats and who starves, who has power and who serves, who can choose their life and who has no choice at all. It shapes what people do all day, what they aspire to, and what they're willing to sacrifice.

✦

## In This Section

### Concepts & Frameworks

- [A Note Before You Begin](#s4-intro)
- [How Economics Works in Romantasy](#s4-frameworks)
- [Common Patterns](#s4-patterns)
- [Common Mistakes](#s4-mistakes)

### Part One: The Foundation

- [Economic Systems](#s4-economic-systems)
- [Currency & Scale](#s4-currency)
- [Work & Occupations](#s4-work)
- [Wealth & Class](#s4-wealth)
- [Resources & Scarcity](#s4-resources)

### Part Two: Economy & Romance

- [Your Characters' Economic Positions](#s4-positions)
- [The Wealth Gap Dynamic](#s4-wealth-gap)
- [Money, Gifts & What They Mean](#s4-gifts)
- [Debt, Obligation & Economic Coercion](#s4-debt)
- [Labor & Occupation in Romance](#s4-labor)
- [Economic Sacrifice](#s4-sacrifice)
- [Quick Reference](#s4-quick-ref)

*This section is divided into two parts:*

**Part One: The Foundation** — How the economy actually works. What people do for a living, how wealth is created and distributed, what's valuable and what's scarce. This is the economic reality your characters live in regardless of their romantic situation.

**Part Two: Economy & Romance** — How economic realities create romantic tension. Power imbalances between lovers, financial pressures that force impossible choices, what money means when it passes between people who care about each other.

You need both.

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question will apply to your story. **Skip what doesn't.**

**If your world mirrors a historical economy, focus on what's different.** Focus on what deviates from reader expectations and what specifically creates tension for your romance.

**Economics can stay in the background.** If your obstacles are political, magical, or cultural rather than economic, develop just enough economy to make the world feel real.

## How Economics Works in Romantasy

Economics in romantasy typically creates tension through **imbalance**, **pressure**, and **transaction**. Money isn't just money — it's power, security, freedom, and control.

> #### Economic Imbalance
>
> When one character has significantly more wealth than the other:
>
> - Power dynamics shift — who depends on whom?
> - Generosity can feel like control
> - Pride and shame enter the relationship
> - The question "do they love me or my money?" haunts the wealthy

> #### Economic Pressure
>
> When financial necessity forces choices:
>
> - Arranged marriages for money or alliance
> - Accepting employment from a love interest
> - Choosing security over love (or love over security)
> - Debt creating obligation

> #### Economic Transaction
>
> When money or resources pass between lovers:
>
> - Gifts carry meaning beyond their value
> - Help can feel like charity
> - Refusal can feel like rejection
> - "I owe you" creates imbalance

## Common Patterns: Economics in Romantasy

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The wealth gap** | One character rich, one poor | Power imbalance; questions about motives |
| **The economic rescue** | Wealthy character can solve poor character's problems | Gratitude vs. obligation; "am I being bought?" |
| **The arrangement** | Financial necessity forces proximity | Forced proximity; "is this real?" |
| **The debt** | One owes the other (money, life, service) | Obligation; when is the debt paid? |
| **The sacrifice** | One gives up wealth/security for love | High stakes; proving love isn't about money |
| **The inheritance threat** | Being together means losing fortune | External pressure; choosing love or security |

## Common Mistakes with Economic Worldbuilding

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #1: The Meaningless Wealth Gap
>
> **What it looks like:** One character is rich and one is poor, but it never affects anything. The poor character has no financial pressures.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Economic difference creates stakes only when it has real consequences.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Make the gap mean something. The poor character faces real scarcity. The rich character's money affects the power dynamic.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #2: The Infinite Wealth
>
> **What it looks like:** The wealthy character can buy their way out of any problem. Money is never actually a constraint.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Stakes require constraints. If wealth solves everything, the story loses tension.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Give wealth limits. Their wealth is tied up, controlled by family, or comes with strings. Or money simply can't solve this particular problem.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #3: The Invisible Economy
>
> **What it looks like:** Characters exist in an economic vacuum. No one seems to work or worry about money.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Ground characters in economic reality. Readers should understand how they survive. Choices have economic consequences.

Section 4 · Part One

# The Foundation

## Economic Systems

#### What type of economy exists in your world?

| --- | --- | --- |

| Mercantile/Trade |  | Wealth from commerce; merchant class rising |
| Guild-Based |  | Professions controlled by guilds; must apprentice |
| Magical Economy |  | Magic is primary source of wealth/production |

## Currency & Scale

#### What's the rough scale of money?

*Readers need to feel the weight of money in your story. What costs would make your protagonist wince, and what would your love interest spend without thinking?*

| Item | Approximate Cost |
| --- | --- |
| Day's unskilled labor wage |  |
| Simple meal |  |
| Night at an inn |  |

| A year's comfortable living |  |
| Day's unskilled labor wage | 5 copper pieces; a skilled craftsman earns 2 silver |

|  |  |
|  |  |

### Currency Design

You don't need to design a complete monetary system. But a few details about currency can make your world feel lived-in.

What's stamped on a coin tells readers something about who's in power. What materials are used tells them what's valued. Perhaps gold coins show the king's face, or copper tokens bear a guild's symbol.

These small choices help readers feel the weight of money when it changes hands in your story.

#### What materials is your currency made from?

*Gold, silver, copper? Magical crystals? Stamped leather? Carved bone? The material itself says something about your world's resources and values.*

[Your answer here]

#### What's depicted on your currency?

*Monarchs' faces? Religious symbols? Mythical creatures? National emblems? Consider what the ruling powers want citizens to see and remember every time they make a transaction.*

[Your answer here]

#### What are your currency denominations called?

*Crowns, sovereigns, dragons, marks, florins? Names often reflect history, rulers, or cultural values. A coin called a "blessing" implies different values than one called a "skull."*

[Your answer here]

#### How do denominations relate to each other?

*10 coppers to a silver? 20 silvers to a gold? Unusual ratios (like 12 or 7) can make your world feel distinct.*

[Your answer here]

#### Design Your Currency

*Use this table to detail your currency system.*

| Denomination | Material | Value | What's Depicted |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| Crown | Gold | 100 coppers | Reigning monarch on front, royal crest on back |
| Talon | Silver | 10 coppers | Griffin clutching a sword |
| Bit | Copper | Base unit | Sheaf of wheat |

## Work & Occupations

### Reference: Fantasy Professions & Jobs

*Use this list for inspiration when filling in occupation tables. What work shapes your characters' daily lives?*

| Category | Examples |
| --- | --- |
| **Nobility & Governance** | Lord/lady, steward, seneschal, ambassador, tax collector, herald, court advisor, magistrate |
| **Military & Combat** | Knight, soldier, guard, mercenary, assassin, archer, cavalry, siege engineer, weapons master |
| **Skilled Trades** | Blacksmith, carpenter, mason, tailor, cobbler, jeweler, glassblower, tanner, cooper, wheelwright |
| **Merchants & Trade** | Shopkeeper, trader, caravan master, ship captain, money-lender, auctioneer, innkeeper, tavern owner |
| **Service & Household** | Servant, maid, butler, cook, groom, lady's maid, valet, housekeeper, gardener, steward |
| **Food & Agriculture** | Farmer, shepherd, miller, baker, butcher, brewer, vintner, fisherman, beekeeper, hunter |
| **Healing & Medicine** | Healer, midwife, herbalist, apothecary, physician, surgeon, plague doctor, dentist |
| **Religious & Spiritual** | Priest, priestess, monk, nun, temple keeper, oracle, shrine maiden, inquisitor, exorcist |
| **Magical Professions** | Court mage, hedge witch, enchanter, potion-maker, diviner, ward-smith, spell-scribe, familiar handler |
| **Arts & Entertainment** | Bard, musician, actor, painter, sculptor, storyteller, jester, acrobat, dancer, playwright |
| **Scholarly & Learned** | Scribe, scholar, tutor, librarian, historian, cartographer, astronomer, alchemist, translator |
| **Underworld & Shadows** | Thief, pickpocket, smuggler, fence, spy, informant, bounty hunter, poisoner, tomb robber |

**How to write it:** Work leaves marks. Show occupation through a character's body (callused hands, ink-stained fingers, the way they stand), their knowledge (what they notice, what's invisible to others), and their habits (reflexive actions learned through repetition). A blacksmith notices metalwork quality; a merchant calculates value automatically; a soldier assesses threat. When characters from different work worlds meet, let them discover each other through these competencies. There's intimacy in watching someone do what they're good at — and in being seen doing what you're good at by someone who appreciates it.

#### What professions are respected? Looked down upon?

*Occupational status shapes how people are treated and what doors open or close. If your characters have different occupations, how might society view their pairing?*

| Status | Occupations |
| --- | --- |
| Highly respected |  |
| Respectable/ordinary |  |
| Necessary but low-status |  |
| Disreputable or shameful |  |
| Highly respected | Court mages, master healers, high priests, landed nobility |
| Disreputable or shameful | Hedge witches, fortune-tellers, gravediggers, money-lenders |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

## Resources & Scarcity

> *Building on Section 1
>
> Your Resource Foundation
>
> In [Section 1: Resources & Territorial Control](01_Geography_Environment.html#s1-resources), you mapped where resources are found and who controls them. Now we'll explore how that control creates economic stakes and romantic tension.*

#### What's the single most valuable resource in your world?

*The thing people would kill for, die for, or sell their freedom for:*

[Your answer here]

### Natural Resources → Culture Impact

What a place has shapes who its people become. A coastal village lives differently than a mining town.

You don't need to catalog every resource in your world. Just think about the one or two things that define your main setting. Perhaps it's a forest kingdom where woodcraft is sacred, or a desert city built on control of the only water source.

These resources create the backdrop for your characters' daily lives — and often, the source of their conflicts.

#### How do your world's primary resources shape local cultures?

*Consider how abundance or scarcity of certain resources creates distinct traditions, crafts, and ways of life.*

| Resource | How It Shapes Culture |
| --- | --- |
| Timber | Carpentry valued, forests sacred, wooden architecture dominates |
| Gold/Precious metals | Mining economy, ornate craftsmanship, wealth displays define status |
| Wool/Textiles | Weaving traditions passed down, nomadic herders, vibrant textile markets |
| Grain/Agriculture | Harvest festivals central to calendar, farming cycles dictate life, land ownership matters deeply |
| Salt | Preservation culture, salt mining communities, unique cuisine and trade routes |
| Fish/Sea | Coastal culture, sailing traditions, sea worship, ports as cultural hubs |
| Gemstones | Jewelry crafting traditions, mining towns, gem-based status symbols |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

# Economy & Romance

## Your Characters' Economic Positions

#### Where does your protagonist fall economically?

*Their economic position shapes what they worry about, what they dream of, and what they would sacrifice for security or freedom.*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Economic status (wealthy, comfortable, struggling, poor) |  |
| Source of income/survival |  |
| Economic security (stable, precarious, desperate) |  |
| Economic autonomy (independent, dependent, controlled) |  |

#### Where does your love interest fall economically?

*How does their economic reality differ from your protagonist's? What assumptions do they make about money that might clash or align?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Economic status |  |
| Source of income/survival |  |
| Economic security |  |
| Economic autonomy |  |

## The Wealth Gap Dynamic

If your characters have different economic positions, that tension runs deeper than "one has money, one doesn't." The most interesting wealth-gap romances don't resolve this by pretending money doesn't matter. They work through it.

**How to write it:** Show the gap through small moments more than big gestures. The wealthy character casually ordering something the other couldn't afford in a month. The poor character flinching at prices. Different assumptions about what's "normal" — having multiple coats, eating meat daily, never worrying about next month's rent. Show both directions: the wealthy character's obliviousness (not unkind, just inexperienced), and the poor character's knowledge the wealthy one lacks (how to stretch food, read a room for danger, survive without protection). When wealth difference creates friction, don't resolve it quickly — let characters struggle with pride, obligation, and the way money complicates everything. The best wealth-gap romances make readers feel both sides.

#### What can the wealthier character provide that the other can't access alone?

*Safety? Comfort? Opportunity? Legal protection? Social access?*

[Your answer here]

#### What does the less wealthy character bring that money can't buy?

*Skills? Knowledge? Magical ability? Street smarts? Moral clarity?*

[Your answer here]

#### How does the wealth gap affect their power dynamic?

*Does one depend on the other? Is there resentment? Does the wealthy one use money as control?*

[Your answer here]

#### How do they each feel about the gap?

*Shame? Guilt? Obliviousness? Resentment? These feelings may never be spoken aloud, but they shape every interaction.*

| Character | How They Feel About the Gap |
| --- | --- |
| Wealthier character |  |
| Less wealthy character |  |

## Money, Gifts & What They Mean

When money or expensive gifts pass between people who care about each other, it's never simple. For the giver, a gift can be an expression of care — or a substitute for harder things. For the receiver, it can feel like love made tangible — or like charity.

**How to write it:** The tension in financial offers is in the moment of decision. Slow down when one character offers and the other must respond. Show the internal calculation: the receiver weighing need against pride, wondering what accepting means. Show the giver noticing this hesitation, perhaps trying to make it easier — or accidentally making it worse. The words matter: "Let me help" versus "You need this" versus "It's nothing" carry different weights. After an offer is accepted or refused, show the aftermath: the lingering awkwardness, the shift in how they see each other. A refused offer can wound the giver; an accepted offer can bind the receiver. Neither is simple.

#### If the wealthier character offers to pay for something, how does the other feel?

*Grateful? Humiliated? Obligated? Suspicious of motives?*

[Your answer here]

#### What financial gesture would be genuinely romantic — not transactional?

*Not buying something expensive, but noticing what they need and providing it without being asked. The gesture that says "I see you" rather than "I can afford this."*

[Your answer here]

## Debt, Obligation & Economic Coercion

Debt creates a relationship before there is a relationship. The problem with debt in romance is that it clouds consent.

#### Is either character in debt?

*Debt creates obligation, and obligation clouds consent. Does debt to the love interest (or a third party) complicate the romance?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| To whom |  |
| For what (money, life saved, magical service) |  |
| What happens if they can't repay |  |

## Labor & Occupation in Romance

What people do for work isn't just how they earn money — it's how they spend most of their waking hours, what skills they develop, and often how they see themselves.

#### What does your protagonist do to survive?

*Work shapes who we become. What skills, knowledge, and worldview has their occupation given them?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Occupation |  |
| Skills learned through work |  |

#### What does your love interest do to survive?

*How does their work connect to or conflict with your protagonist's world? Do their occupations create proximity or distance?*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Occupation |  |
| Skills learned through work |  |

#### How do their occupations bring them together or keep them apart?

*Same workplace? Completely separate worlds? Does one employ the other?*

[Your answer here]

## Economic Sacrifice

One of romance's most powerful beats is the moment a character gives up material security for love. Economic sacrifice proves love is real in a way words cannot.

**How to write it:** Make the sacrifice concrete and visible. Not just "gave up the inheritance" but: the smaller apartment, the mended clothes, the dinner that's mostly bread. Show what daily life looks like after the sacrifice — the moments of regret, the moments of fierce certainty that it was worth it. The witness to this sacrifice matters: when the love interest realizes what their partner gave up, or when the sacrificer reveals it (or carefully doesn't reveal it, and the other discovers it anyway). Economic sacrifice is most powerful when it's ongoing, not a one-time grand gesture — it's choosing, every day, to live with less because what you gained matters more.

#### What economic security does each character sacrifice to be together?

*What inheritance, income, protection, or opportunity do they lose by choosing each other? The sacrifice should cost enough to prove the love is real.*

| Character | What They Sacrifice |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

## Quick Reference: Economic Stakes

| Element | Your World |
| --- | --- |
| Type of economy |  |
| Primary economic obstacle for romance |  |
| Economic power imbalance |  |
| The valuable/scarce resource |  |
| Economic debt or obligation |  |
| Economic sacrifice required to be together |  |

> *Final Thought
>
> Money Gets Personal
>
> The question isn't "how does the tax system work?" — it's "what would this character do if they couldn't eat tomorrow?" Economics creates stakes when it forces impossible choices: Love or survival? Pride or security? Build an economy that makes these choices real.*

Section 5

# Government, Power & Law

Politics, law, forbidden relationships, enemies-to-lovers

Who holds power? How did they get it? What laws govern your world? These questions matter because political structures create the obstacles, stakes, and pressures that shape your characters' choices.

This section is divided into two parts:

> #### Part One: The Foundation
>
> How power actually works in your world. Forms of government, how laws are made and enforced, how political power intersects with magic. This is the political reality your characters live under regardless of their romantic situation.

> #### Part Two: Politics & Romance
>
> How political realities create romantic tension. Laws that forbid unions, arranged marriages, enemies across battle lines, the stakes of being caught. This builds on the foundation to show how your world's politics specifically affects your love story.

**You need both.**

## Types of Government Reference

You don't need to invent a government from scratch. Most fantasy worlds draw on familiar types, and that's a strength, not a weakness.

Here's a quick reference to get you started. Think about which one fits your world—or mix and match. The right choice depends on what conflicts and constraints you want your characters to navigate.

| Type | Description | Romantic Implications |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Monarchy** | King/Queen rules, power by bloodline | Succession drama, forbidden love across ranks |
| **Empire** | Multiple states under one emperor | Characters from different conquered regions |
| **Oligarchy** | Small group of wealthy/powerful rule | Family alliances, marriage as power play |
| **Republic** | Elected representatives make laws | Political marriages, faction rivalries |
| **Theocracy** | Religious leaders hold power | Forbidden love, duty vs desire |
| **Federation** | States united under central government | Regional loyalties, border tensions |

#### Which government type(s) does your world use?

*Your world might use one type, combine elements from several, or have different systems in different regions.*

[Your answer here]

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question will apply to your story. **Skip what doesn't.**

**If politics is background, keep it simple.** Not every romantasy needs complex political intrigue. Develop just enough to make the world feel real.

**Politics can stay vague.** Some stories work better with political structures that are felt rather than detailed.

# Part One: The Foundation

## Forms of Government

Different political structures create different pressures, different power dynamics, and different possibilities for your characters.

### Government Types

#### What form of government exists?

*The form of government determines who has power to make your characters' lives easier or harder, and what paths exist to change unjust laws.*

| Type | How Power Works | Common Features |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Absolute Monarchy** | Single ruler with unchecked power | Succession crises, court intrigue, ruler's whim is law |
| **Constitutional Monarchy** | Ruler limited by laws/councils | Balance of power, competing factions, reform possible |
| **Feudal System** | Hierarchy of lords owing loyalty upward | Local power varies, divided loyalties, regional differences |
| **Theocracy** | Religious authority rules | Divine law, religious hierarchy holds power, heresy is treason |
| **Magocracy** | Magical power determines rule | Non-mages marginalized, magical bloodlines matter |

### Government Types (continued)

| Type | How Power Works | Common Features |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Oligarchy** | Small group holds power (nobles, merchants, mages) | Factional conflict, closed system, hard to break in |
| **Republic/Democracy** | Elected or representative rule | Popular opinion matters, elections as pressure points |
| **Empire** | Central power over conquered territories | Core vs. periphery, colonial dynamics, rebellion threats |
| **Tribal/Clan** | Power through kinship groups | Family loyalty paramount, inter-clan politics |
| **Council Rule** | Decisions by committee | Consensus needed, gridlock possible, coalition building |

#### Which describes your world?

*You can combine elements or note regional differences. Consider how this system creates obstacles for your romance.*

[Your answer here]

#### If your world has multiple nations, do they have different systems?

*Political differences between nations can separate lovers or offer escape routes. Where would your characters flee if they had to?*

[Your answer here]

## Political Structure & Hierarchy

**How to write it:** Show hierarchy through behavior, not explanation. Who waits for whom? Who speaks first? Who sits while others stand? Who can interrupt and who must be silent? The physical choreography of power — where people position themselves in a room, who makes eye contact with whom — reveals hierarchy without a word of exposition. When your characters occupy different positions in the hierarchy, every interaction carries the weight of that difference: the higher-ranked character's casual assumptions, the lower-ranked character's constant navigation. Romantic tension in hierarchical gaps comes from moments when the hierarchy falters — when the powerful one asks instead of commands, when the lower one refuses to defer.

#### What's the political hierarchy?

*Map it from highest to lowest power:*

[Your answer here]

#### How do people move up (or down) in political power?

*Birth? Achievement? Magical ability? Wealth? Military service? Marriage?*

[Your answer here]

*Focus on positions that your characters hold, aspire to, or must navigate around. Who could grant them permission or protection?*

| Position | Power They Hold | Who Currently Holds It |
| --- | --- | --- |

|  |  |  |

# Part Two: Politics & Romance

## How to Think About Politics in Romantasy

Politics in romantasy functions as **external pressure** — forces beyond your characters' control that forbid, compel, or complicate their relationship. Unlike internal obstacles (fear, trust issues, personal history), political obstacles exist whether your characters want them to or not.

Political pressure works on romance in three ways:

> #### Politics as Prohibition
>
> Laws and political structures that forbid the relationship:
>
> - Marriage laws that make their union illegal
> - Class systems that make their relationship scandalous
> - Species laws that criminalize their love
> - Political enemies who cannot be seen to fraternize

> #### Politics as Compulsion
>
> Political forces that push characters together against their will:
>
> - Arranged marriages for alliance or peace
> - Political necessity requiring cooperation
> - Shared enemies creating forced alliance
> - Diplomatic situations demanding proximity

> #### Politics as Stakes
>
> What characters risk by pursuing the relationship:
>
> - Execution, imprisonment, exile for illegal unions
> - Loss of title, inheritance, political position
> - War, broken treaties, political chaos
> - Harm to family, faction, or nation

The most compelling political obstacles are ones characters can't simply ignore or talk their way out of. The law doesn't care that they're in love. The war doesn't pause for their feelings. The political marriage is happening whether they want it or not.

## Common Patterns: Politics in Romantasy

### Political Situations That Create Romance

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The forbidden union** | Law prohibits their marriage | Secrecy, risk, "us against the world" |
| **The arranged marriage** | Political necessity forces them together | Forced proximity, duty vs. desire |
| **The enemies** | Their peoples/factions are in conflict | Loyalty tested, betrayal required |
| **The political prize** | One is valuable for political marriage | Competition, commodification, rescue |
| **The hostage/ward** | One is held by the other's people | Power imbalance, Stockholm dynamics |

### Political Situations (continued)

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The succession crisis** | Their relationship affects who rules | High stakes, public scrutiny |
| **The peace treaty** | Their union could end a war | Pressure to perform, symbol vs. person |

### Political Character Pairings

| Pairing | Built-in Tension | Key Question |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Royal + Commoner** | Class law, succession, scandal | Can love override duty to bloodline? |
| **Enemy nations** | War, loyalty, treason | Can they choose each other over their people? |
| **Conqueror + Conquered** | Power imbalance, historical wrong | Can there be love without freedom? |
| **Political rivals** | Competing goals, zero-sum game | What would they sacrifice to win — or to love? |
| **Arranged spouses** | Obligation without choice | When does duty become desire? |
| **Guard + Protected** | Professional boundary, power difference | Can duty and love coexist? |

## Common Mistakes with Political Worldbuilding

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #1: The Toothless Law
>
> **What it looks like:** There's a law against their relationship, but no one enforces it. They're "forbidden" but face no real consequences.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Forbidden love only matters if the forbidding has teeth.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Show enforcement. Someone else has been punished. Getting caught would mean concrete, serious consequences.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #2: The Easily Escaped Arrangement
>
> **What it looks like:** One character is in an arranged marriage, but they can simply refuse, or the other betrothed is cartoonishly evil so breaking the arrangement is obviously right.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Arranged marriage tension requires the arrangement to be genuinely binding.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Make the arrangement matter. Breaking it means war, family ruin, or personal destruction. Make the choice genuinely hard.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #3: The Vague Politics
>
> **What it looks like:** There's political conflict, but it's unclear what anyone wants, what the stakes are, or why it matters.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** If readers don't understand the political situation, they can't feel the pressure.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Be specific. Who wants what? Why can't both sides have it? Ground the conflict in concrete goals and consequences.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #4: The Politics That Pause for Romance
>
> **What it looks like:** Serious political conflicts somehow stop mattering when the romance heats up. External pressure disappears when it's inconvenient.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** It breaks immersion and makes stakes feel fake.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Politics should intensify during the romance, not pause. The pressure doesn't let up just because they're falling in love.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #5: The Unearned Enemy-to-Lover
>
> **What it looks like:** Characters are supposed to be enemies, but their enmity is based on misunderstanding or conflict that evaporates once they talk.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** True enemies-to-lovers requires genuine enmity that must be overcome, not misunderstanding to be cleared up.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Build real reasons for enmity. Then make them reckon with that, not just discover they were wrong.

## Power & Magic

If magic exists, it must affect politics. Who has magic affects who has power.

## Marriage Laws

In romantasy, marriage law is often the primary legal obstacle. Understanding what's legal, what's forbidden, and what the consequences are tells you exactly what your characters are up against.

Marriage laws exist for reasons — usually to preserve power structures, control inheritance, maintain class or species boundaries, or serve religious doctrine. Knowing *why* the law exists helps you understand how rigid it is and what challenging it would mean.

#### Who can legally marry whom?

*Consider restrictions based on class, species, magical status, gender, family, religion, age, political status:*

[Your answer here]

#### Why do these restrictions exist?

*Give a reason — even if it's a bad one. Magical danger? Political control? Religious doctrine? Maintaining class system? Historical catastrophe blamed on forbidden unions?*

[Your answer here]

#### What makes a marriage legal and binding?

*Religious ceremony? Legal contract? Magical binding? Consummation? Public declaration? Parental consent? Royal approval?*

[Your answer here]

#### Can marriages be dissolved?

*Divorce allowed? Annulment possible? Magical bonds breakable? Or is marriage permanent?*

[Your answer here]

#### What are the consequences of an illegal marriage?

*Criminal (execution, imprisonment, exile)? Social (loss of title, disownment)? Magical (loss of power, curse)? Political (loss of succession rights, treaty violation)?*

[Your answer here]

## Class & Caste Systems

Class systems create some of romantasy's most enduring obstacles — the prince and the servant, the noble and the commoner. These systems work as romantic obstacles because they're structural, not personal.

What makes class gaps compelling is the different worldviews — the noble has never worried about where their next meal comes from; the servant has never had the luxury of boredom. These experiential gaps create misunderstanding, fascination, and friction.

Class shapes identity in ways that don't disappear when you fall in love. The servant who marries the prince doesn't stop being someone who grew up serving. The most interesting class-gap romances show characters navigating real gaps without pretending the worlds are the same.

#### What social hierarchy exists?

*Map it from highest to lowest, including what determines class (birth, wealth, magic, achievement):*

[Your answer here]

#### Where do your characters fall?

*The gap between them creates the romantic tension. What does each take for granted that the other has never experienced?*

| Character | Class Position | Example (Serpent & Dove) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  | *Witch (criminal class) — beneath even peasants in the eyes of law, hunted and killed on sight* |
| Love Interest |  | *Chasseur Captain — military-religious elite, second only to the Archbishop, respected and feared* |

#### How many ranks separate them?

*Same class? One or two apart (scandalous but navigable)? Extreme gap (servant/royal)?*

[Your answer here]

#### What laws enforce class separation?

*Restricted spaces? Forbidden interactions? What happens if someone acts "above their station"?*

[Your answer here]

#### What social consequences exist for cross-class relationships?

*Loss of status? Disownment? Social isolation?*

[Your answer here]

## Species & Race Laws

If multiple intelligent species exist, there are likely laws governing interaction. These laws often create the hardest obstacles — not just social disapproval but fundamental questions about whether such unions are even possible or recognized.

## Political Arrangements: Forced Together

Arranged marriages and political alliances are romantasy staples because they create forced proximity with high stakes. The characters didn't choose each other — but now they must deal with each other.

What makes arranged marriage compelling as a trope is the tension between obligation and desire. They're bound together by duty, politics, or necessity. The question becomes: can something real grow in soil that wasn't chosen?

#### Is one or both characters in an arranged marriage or betrothal?

*Arranged marriages create both obstacles (if to someone else) and opportunities (if to each other) for romantic tension.*

If yes:

#### Who arranged it and why?

*Family (for alliance, wealth, bloodline)? Ruler (for political stability)? Religious authority (fulfilling prophecy)? The characters themselves made a deal?*

[Your answer here]

#### What makes the arrangement binding?

*Legal penalties? Magical oath? Political necessity? Family pressure? No alternative?*

[Your answer here]

#### Who is the arranged partner?

*Are they decent (potential ally), cruel (clear villain), in love with someone else too (shared predicament), or the actual love interest (arranged marriage to lovers)?*

[Your answer here]

#### What happens if the arrangement is broken?

*War? Family ruin? Loss of protection? Magical backlash? Political chaos?*

[Your answer here]

## Already Bound: When Arrangement Is the Starting Point

Sometimes the arranged marriage isn't an obstacle to escape — it's the premise. They're already married. Now what?

This setup creates a different kind of tension. The legal barrier is already gone. They're bound. The obstacle is internal: learning to see each other as people rather than obligations, building trust where there was none, finding genuine feeling in a relationship that began as transaction.

#### If your characters begin the story already bound by political marriage:

#### How long have they been married?

*Just wed? Months? Years of cold distance?*

[Your answer here]

#### What were the circumstances of the wedding?

*Did they meet before? Was one or both unwilling? Was there hope, dread, or resignation?*

[Your answer here]

#### What's their relationship like at the start of the story?

*Strangers? Polite distance? Active hostility? Tentative curiosity?*

[Your answer here]

#### What does their marriage require of them publicly?

*Must they appear united? Produce an heir? Attend functions together? Share chambers?*

[Your answer here]

#### What private boundaries have they established?

*Separate bedrooms? Limited conversation? Rules about what they will and won't share?*

[Your answer here]

#### What begins to shift those boundaries?

*What event, conversation, or realization starts changing how they see each other?*

[Your answer here]

#### What's the obstacle now — if they're already married?

*The legal barrier is gone, but what emotional, political, or personal barrier remains? Trust issues? One is in love with someone else? They're on opposite sides of a conflict? One holds a secret?*

[Your answer here]

#### When does obligation become choice?

*At what point do they stay together because they want to, not because they must?*

[Your answer here]

## Political Marriages & Treaties

When a marriage serves political purposes, the couple becomes symbols as much as people. They're performing for nations, not just for themselves.

Being a political chess piece does something to a person. You learn that your value lies in what you can secure, not who you are. When two political pawns are married to each other, they share something no one else understands — or they resent seeing in each other a reminder of what they've lost.

The question that haunts political marriages is whether genuine feeling can grow in coerced soil. Would they have chosen each other if they'd had a choice? Sometimes the question never gets a satisfying answer, and they have to live with that uncertainty.

#### Is your characters' relationship politically important?

*Politically significant relationships carry weight beyond personal feelings. Their union could end wars or start them.*

If yes:

#### What political problem does their union solve?

*Ends a war? Unites rival houses? Fulfills prophecy? Secures resources?*

[Your answer here]

#### Who benefits from their union? Who's threatened by it?

*Those who benefit will push them together; those threatened will work to tear them apart.*

| Group | Benefit or Threat |
| --- | --- |
| Who wants them together |  |
| Why |  |
| Who wants them apart |  |
| Why |  |

#### What happens if they refuse the political obligation?

*The consequences of refusal determine whether they have a real choice or only an illusion of one.*

[Your answer here]

#### How does political pressure affect their emotional relationship?

*Do they resent being forced together? Does duty make intimacy feel less genuine? Do they fall in love despite the arrangement — or struggle to trust what they feel because of it?*

[Your answer here]

## Enforcement & Consequences

Laws mean nothing without enforcement. If your characters can break laws with no real risk, there are no stakes. The question isn't just "what's illegal?" but "what happens if they're caught?"

#### Who enforces the law?

*Royal guard? City watch? Magical enforcers? Religious authorities? Secret police? Self-enforcing magical contracts?*

[Your answer here]

#### How effective is enforcement?

*Omnipresent (high risk)? Moderate? Weak or corrupt (easy to evade)? Selective (depends who you are)?*

[Your answer here]

#### What happens when your characters are caught?

*Make consequences concrete and escalating. What happens the first time versus repeated offenses?*

| Situation | Consequence |
| --- | --- |
| First offense |  |
| Caught together |  |
| Marry illegally |  |
| Produce children |  |

## Unequal Justice

Laws often apply differently depending on who you are — one might face death while the other faces a slap on the wrist.

Asymmetric risk poisons relationships in subtle ways. When they say "let's take this risk together," they're not actually taking the same risk. The protected partner may not understand what the vulnerable one faces. The vulnerable partner is always calculating, always aware of what happens if they're caught.

For the protected partner who does understand, there's guilt. They benefit from a system that would destroy the person they love. The most honest relationships acknowledge this asymmetry openly.

#### Are laws enforced equally, or does power matter?

*Does the noble face consequences, or only the commoner? Can the magical character escape while the non-magical suffers?*

[Your answer here]

#### If one character risks everything while the other risks little, how do they navigate that?

*Does the protected character understand what the other faces? Do they try to shield them? Does the vulnerable character resent the inequality?*

[Your answer here]

#### Could the protected character sacrifice their protection for the other?

*Renounce their status? Confess to share punishment? What would that cost?*

[Your answer here]

#### What would happen if only one of them were caught?

*Would the protected character let the other suffer alone? Would they come forward? Would they have the power to save them?*

[Your answer here]

## Diplomatic Protection & Special Status

Some positions grant immunity from normal laws — or make someone untouchable in ways that affect the relationship.

#### Does either character have special legal status?

*Diplomatic immunity? Religious sanctuary? Royal protection? Magical immunity? Status as hostage or ward?*

[Your answer here]

#### How does this status affect what they can do — or what can be done to them?

*Special status can be both shield and cage. What freedoms does it grant, and what constraints does it impose?*

[Your answer here]

#### Could their status protect the other character?

*Could marriage, alliance, or association extend protection to their partner?*

[Your answer here]

#### Could their status endanger the other character?

*Does being connected to them make their partner a target? Create political complications?*

[Your answer here]

#### What would they lose if they gave up their special status?

*Could they renounce it? At what cost?*

[Your answer here]

## Factions & Opposition

Your couple doesn't exist in a vacuum. Political factions may support or oppose them based on how the relationship affects larger power struggles.

#### What major factions exist?

*Focus on factions that would care about your characters' relationship, either to support or destroy it.*

| Faction | Goal |
| --- | --- |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |
|  |  |

*Factional loyalty creates another layer of obligation and potential betrayal in the romance.*

| Character | Faction | Example (Fourth Wing) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  | *Riders Quadrant — elite dragon-bonded warriors who protect Navarre* |
| Love Interest |  | *Riders Quadrant (officially) — but secretly connected to those questioning leadership's lies* |

#### Are they on the same side, opposite sides, or unaligned?

*Same side creates shared purpose; opposite sides creates the richest enemies-to-lovers tension.*

[Your answer here]

#### If on opposite sides, what would it take to betray their faction for each other?

*The moment of choosing love over loyalty is one of the most powerful beats in enemies-to-lovers. What would push them to it?*

[Your answer here]

## Warfare & Military

War is the ultimate political pressure. It separates lovers, forces impossible loyalties, and puts characters on opposite sides. War also changes people — characters who have seen combat carry that experience into their relationships.

### The State of Conflict

#### Is your world at war, at peace, or somewhere in between?

*The state of conflict shapes what's possible for your characters and what risks they face being together.*

- Active warfare (battles happening now)
- Cold war (hostility without open conflict)
- Fragile peace (war recently ended or about to begin)
- Long peace (generations without major conflict)
- Perpetual low-level conflict (border skirmishes, raids, never fully resolved)

[Your answer here]

#### If at war: who's fighting whom, and why?

*Understanding the conflict helps you understand what choosing each other would cost your characters.*

[Your answer here]

#### If at peace: what threatens to break it? What tensions simmer beneath the surface?

*Fragile peace creates its own tension. Could your characters' relationship tip the balance toward war or lasting peace?*

[Your answer here]

#### How does the state of conflict affect daily life?

*Rationing? Curfews? Suspicion of outsiders? Refugees? Economic strain? Absent family members?*

[Your answer here]

### Military Service

#### Who serves in the military?

*Volunteers? Conscripts? Nobility obligated to fight? Mercenaries? Magical warriors? A professional standing army?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is military service required?

*For whom? At what age? For how long? Can it be avoided — and how?*

[Your answer here]

#### What's the social status of soldiers?

*Honored? Feared? Pitied? Looked down upon? Does it vary by rank?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is either of your characters in military service?

*Military service creates duty that can conflict with love, and experiences that change a person.*

If yes:

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Which character |  |
| Role/rank |  |
| Why they serve |  |
| What they've seen or done |  |

#### If one character is a soldier, how does this affect the relationship?

*Absence and separation? PTSD and emotional distance? Skills that are attractive or frightening? Duty that conflicts with love?*

[Your answer here]

### Enemies Across Battle Lines

If your characters are on opposite sides of a war, their relationship is treason — choosing each other means betraying everything they were raised to believe in.

#### Are your characters on opposite sides of a conflict?

*Opposite sides creates the highest-stakes enemies-to-lovers setup. Choosing each other means betraying everything.*

If yes:

| Aspect | Protagonist | Love Interest | Example (Serpent & Dove) |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| What conflict |  |  | *The witch hunts — systematic persecution of magic users by the Church* |
| Their side |  |  | *She's a witch (hunted) / He's a Chasseur (hunter)* |

*Propaganda, stereotypes, justified grievances:*

| Side | What They Believe About the Enemy | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Protagonist's side |  | *Chasseurs are murderers who burn innocents; the Church is built on lies and hate* |
| Love interest's side |  | *Witches are agents of darkness; their magic corrupts everything it touches; they must die for humanity to be safe* |

*Traitor? Spy? Corrupted? Foolish? Understandable but forbidden?*

[Your answer here]

*What would they have to believe, learn, or experience?*

[Your answer here]

#### Could their relationship end the conflict — or make it worse?

*Does their union have political implications for the war?*

[Your answer here]

War leaves marks that don't heal.

*Violence changes people. How does this experience affect their capacity for intimacy and trust?*

*Victory for one side? Negotiated peace? Exhaustion? Outside intervention?*

*What would each side have to give up or accept?*
