*Peace doesn't erase memory. Consider whether society would accept them even after conflict ends.*

[Your answer here]

## Enemies to Lovers: Building Genuine Enmity

"Enemies to lovers" is one of the most popular romance tropes — and one of the most frequently underbuilt. Too often, the "enemies" dislike each other for reasons that evaporate the moment attraction becomes undeniable.

Real enemies-to-lovers requires *worldbuilding* that creates genuine enmity. Not misunderstanding. Real, structural reasons why these two people should hate each other.

> **Misunderstanding creates obstacles that dissolve once cleared up. Genuine enmity creates obstacles that require sacrifice, growth, or transformation to overcome.**

What makes enemies-to-lovers satisfying is watching two people reckon with real grievances, not discover they never had any.

### The Foundation: Why Are They Actually Enemies?

#### Which of these creates the conflict between your characters?

*Check all that apply and note specifics:*

**☐ Opposing sides of a war or conflict**
They or their peoples are literally trying to kill each other.

[Your answer here]

**☐ Historical wrongs between their peoples**
One group harmed the other in ways that haven't been forgiven.

[Your answer here]

**☐ Directly opposed goals**
What one wants requires destroying what the other wants.

[Your answer here]

**☐ One has power over the other (and has used it)**
Master/slave, conqueror/conquered, oppressor/oppressed.

[Your answer here]

**☐ One has personally harmed the other (or their loved ones)**
Not their peoples — them specifically. Direct personal injury.

[Your answer here]

**☐ Fundamentally opposed beliefs or values**
What one considers moral, the other considers evil.

[Your answer here]

**☐ Competition for something only one can have**
Throne, position, resource, territory — winner takes all.

[Your answer here]

**☐ One represents the system that oppresses the other**
Even if they didn't create it, they benefit from and enforce it.

[Your answer here]

#### How many of these apply to your story?

*More sources of enmity create richer conflict, but also require more careful resolution.*

- **1:** You have a foundation. Consider whether it's strong enough.
- **2-3:** Good complexity. Multiple layers of conflict.
- **4+:** Rich enmity, but make sure it's not insurmountable.

### The Legitimacy Test

#### Could a reasonable person, knowing what your protagonist knows, hate your love interest?

*Not just dislike — *hate*. If the answer is no, your enmity isn't strong enough.*

[Your answer here]

#### Could a reasonable person, knowing what your love interest knows, hate your protagonist?

*Enemies to lovers works best when both have legitimate grievances.*

[Your answer here]

### Types of Political Enmity

#### Historical Enemies

Sometimes the conflict predates your characters — they inherited it.

#### What historical wrong exists between their peoples?

*Conquest? Massacre? Betrayal? Enslavement? Theft of land or magic?*

[Your answer here]

#### Who did what to whom?

*Be specific. Vague "ancient grudge" isn't enough.*

[Your answer here]

#### How recent is this history?

*Living memory? Grandparents' time? Centuries ago but never forgotten?*

[Your answer here]

#### What evidence of this history remains?

*Scars on the land, displaced peoples, stolen artifacts, suppressed culture, ongoing inequality?*

[Your answer here]

#### How does each character relate to this history?

*Do they feel personal guilt/grievance, or do they see it as "the past"?*

| Character | Relationship to Historical Wrong |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Personal Enemies

The most intense enmity: one has directly harmed the other.

#### What did one character do to the other (or their loved ones)?

*Personal harm creates the most intense enmity. Be specific about what happened.*

[Your answer here]

#### Was it intentional, justified (in their mind), or accidental?

*Their understanding of the harm shapes whether and how forgiveness might be possible.*

[Your answer here]

#### Does the one who caused harm feel guilty, justified, or conflicted?

*Guilt opens paths to reconciliation; justification requires the character to change their worldview first.*

[Your answer here]

#### What would justice look like to the wronged party?

*Death? Confession? Reparation? There's no path forward without addressing this.*

[Your answer here]

#### Can this harm actually be forgiven?

*Some things can't be — and pretending otherwise insults readers. If one killed the other's family, "sorry" doesn't cut it.*

[Your answer here]

#### Ideological Enemies

Sometimes they're enemies because they believe fundamentally different things about how the world should work.

#### What does each character believe that the other finds repugnant?

*Ideological conflict requires characters to reckon with genuinely opposing values, not just misunderstanding.*

| Character | Belief the Other Finds Repugnant |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Why does each believe this?

*Not just "they were raised that way" — what experiences, evidence, or reasoning supports their view?*

| Character | Reasoning Behind Their Belief |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Is either belief clearly wrong?

*If so, the story is about one character learning they were wrong — which is fine, but different from true ideological conflict. If both views have merit, the story is about synthesis or accepting difference — more complex.*

[Your answer here]

### The Path from Enemies to Lovers

The journey from enmity to love requires specific beats: the crack in assumptions, the glimpse of complexity, the understanding that doesn't mean agreement, the choice that costs something.

#### The Crack in the Armor

At some point, one character does something that doesn't fit the other's understanding of who they are.

#### What does the love interest do that surprises the protagonist?

*An act of kindness, mercy, honor, or vulnerability they weren't expecting:*

[Your answer here]

#### How does the protagonist explain this to themselves initially?

*Rationalize it? Distrust it? Assume ulterior motive?*

[Your answer here]

#### What does the protagonist do that surprises the love interest?

*An act of honor, mercy, or kindness that doesn't fit the enemy stereotype.*

[Your answer here]

#### How does the love interest initially explain this?

*Denial, suspicion of manipulation, or cognitive dissonance are common first reactions.*

[Your answer here]

#### Seeing Through Their Eyes

The turning point in enemies-to-lovers is usually when one character genuinely understands the other's perspective — not agrees with it, but *understands* why they believe what they believe.

#### What does the protagonist learn about why the love interest is the way they are?

*Not excuses — context. The experiences, pressures, or reasoning behind their position.*

[Your answer here]

#### How does this knowledge change the protagonist's view?

*They don't have to agree. But do they understand?*

[Your answer here]

#### What does the love interest learn about the protagonist's perspective?

*Context that reveals the protagonist's position isn't simple villainy but complex circumstance.*

[Your answer here]

#### How does this change them?

*Understanding doesn't require agreement, but it should shift how they see the conflict.*

[Your answer here]

#### The Point of No Return

At some point, each character has to choose the relationship over their "side." This is the real test.

#### What does the protagonist do that their side would consider betrayal?

*Not just "has feelings" — an action that crosses a line.*

[Your answer here]

#### What does this cost them?

*Real sacrifice makes the choice meaningful. What do they give up that they can never get back?*

[Your answer here]

#### What does the love interest do that their side would consider betrayal?

*The mirror moment: their matching act of choosing love over loyalty.*

[Your answer here]

#### What does this cost them?

*The cost should be commensurate with the protagonist's sacrifice.*

[Your answer here]

### Reckoning with the Enmity

At some point, they have to actually address the reason they were enemies. Not avoid it. Not "that's in the past." Actually talk about it.

#### The Conversation That Has to Happen

#### What specifically needs to be addressed?

*The historical wrong, the personal injury, the ideological divide — name it:*

[Your answer here]

#### Who brings it up?

*The one who was wronged? The one who caused harm? Does it happen naturally or get forced?*

[Your answer here]

#### What does each character need to say?

| Character | What They Need to Say |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### What does each need to hear?

| Character | What They Need to Hear |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Is there an apology? Is it sufficient?

*Words alone may not be enough. What actions accompany the apology to make it real?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is there forgiveness? Is it earned?

*Unearned forgiveness feels false to readers. What makes forgiveness possible here?*

[Your answer here]

#### What Can't Be Fixed

Some harms can't be undone. Some losses are permanent. Good enemies-to-lovers acknowledges this.

#### What damage from their enmity is permanent?

*Lives lost, time wasted, trust that can never be fully restored:*

[Your answer here]

#### How do they live with what can't be fixed?

*Acceptance isn't forgetting. How do they honor what was lost while building something new?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is there anything one can do to make amends — even if it can't undo the harm?

*Amends isn't about erasing the past but about what they do with the future.*

[Your answer here]

### The Stakes of Reconciliation

#### If they choose each other, what does the protagonist lose?

*Community, family, identity, safety, purpose:*

[Your answer here]

#### What does the love interest lose?

*Parallel sacrifice creates balance. They should both give up something significant.*

[Your answer here]

#### Are these losses worth it?

*The answer should be "yes, but barely" — the cost should be real enough that choosing each other means something.*

[Your answer here]

### World Impact

#### Does their relationship affect the larger conflict?

*Symbol of peace? Proof that reconciliation is possible? Catalyst for change? Or just two people who found each other despite everything?*

[Your answer here]

#### How do others react to their relationship?

| Side | Reaction |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist's side |  |
| Love interest's side |  |

### Common Enemies-to-Lovers Pitfalls

Use this as a checklist to make sure your enmity is strong enough:

#### Check: Is your enmity based primarily on misunderstanding?

*If clearing up one misunderstanding would dissolve the conflict, you don't have enemies-to-lovers — you have miscommunication romance. That's fine, but it's a different trope.*

[Your answer here]

#### Check: Does attraction make them forget their legitimate grievances?

*Enemies shouldn't stop being enemies just because they find each other hot. Physical attraction while maintaining genuine conflict is part of the tension.*

[Your answer here]

#### Check: Do they ever actually address the reason they were enemies?

*If they just fall in love and the conflict is never discussed, resolved, or reckoned with, the ending feels unearned.*

[Your answer here]

#### Check: Does falling in love make either character unrecognizable?

*They can change, but they shouldn't become entirely different people. The stern commander can soften without becoming a puppy.*

[Your answer here]

## Succession & Heirs

If your characters are part of powerful families, succession affects the romance. Who they marry, whether they produce heirs, and what bloodlines they mix all have political consequences.

#### What does each character stand to inherit?

*Inheritance creates stakes beyond the personal. Their choices may affect generations.*

| Character | Inheritance |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### How does their relationship affect inheritance?

*Marrying wrong costs inheritance? They must marry to inherit? Their union combines inheritances?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is either expected to produce an heir?

*Heir expectations add pressure and create conflict if the relationship makes heirs impossible or complicated.*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Timeline |  |
| What happens if they don't |  |

#### If they're different species or one is sterile, how does this affect heir requirements?

*Biological complications can be devastating when bloodlines are politically essential.*

[Your answer here]

#### Could their relationship cause or solve a succession crisis?

*High-stakes inheritance drama raises the pressure on their relationship considerably.*

[Your answer here]

## Revolution & Reform

Sometimes the system itself is the antagonist. The laws are unjust. The government is oppressive. The only way to be together is to change the world.

#### Are there legal means to change the laws keeping them apart?

*Appealing for exception? Changing laws politically? Waiting for a new ruler? Finding loopholes?*

[Your answer here]

#### If peaceful change is impossible, what would revolution look like?

[Your answer here]

#### Would your characters join or lead a revolution?

*What would push them from surviving the system to fighting it?*

[Your answer here]

#### What would they sacrifice to change the system?

*Safety? Family? Status? Each other?*

[Your answer here]

#### What's lost even if they win?

*Revolution isn't clean. Lives lost? Stability destroyed? Becoming what they fought?*

[Your answer here]

## Integration Check

| Question | Answer | Example (Serpent & Dove) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| How does your government control, use, or fear magic? |  | *The Church hunts and executes witches; magic is seen as demonic corruption; Chasseurs are the enforcement arm* |
| What magical law most affects your characters? |  | *Witchcraft is a capital crime — she's guilty by birth, not action. Merely existing makes her an outlaw* |
| Could magic let them bypass political obstacles? If yes, why don't they? |  | *She could enchant her way out, but it would prove she's a witch. Using magic to escape means proving she deserves to die* |
| Does government have magical enforcement (truth spells, tracking, binding oaths)? |  | *Blessings that supposedly detect witches; holy ground that's meant to repel evil; none of it actually works, but belief in it is powerful* |

## The Core Political Obstacle

> *Key Question
>
> In one sentence, what is the primary political obstacle keeping your characters apart?*

[Your answer here]

#### How can this obstacle be overcome?

*Consider what's possible in your world and what fits your characters' values.*

- Changing who they are (gaining status, power, different identity)
- Changing the system (reform or revolution)
- Escaping the system (running away, exile)
- Accepting consequences (being together despite the cost)

#### Which path do your characters take?

*The path they choose reveals their character and shapes the story's themes.*

[Your answer here]

#### When does the political obstacle force a choice?

*The crisis point when they can no longer avoid confronting it:*

[Your answer here]

## Quick Reference: Political Stakes

| Element | Your World | Example (Serpent & Dove-style) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| The law(s) that forbid the romance |  | *Witches are to be burned; she IS a witch. Their marriage itself is illegal.* |
| Consequences if caught |  | *Execution for her; disgrace and exile for him as a witch-hunter who wed one* |
| Who enforces and how aggressively |  | *The Church's Chasseurs — hunters trained from childhood to kill her kind* |
| Political enemy who opposes them |  | *The Archbishop — his adoptive father, who will personally light the pyre* |
| Political ally who supports them |  | *Fellow hunters who look the other way; witches who owe her debts* |
| State of conflict (war/peace) |  | *Cold war — witches hide, hunters seek. Open war is brewing.* |

### Quick Reference (continued)

| Element | Your World | Example (Serpent & Dove-style) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Military obligations affecting the romance |  | *He's sworn to hunt witches. She's promised to kill the Archbishop.* |
| Source of genuine enmity (if enemies-to-lovers) |  | *His people burned her mother. Her people have killed hunters he loved.* |
| What each loses by choosing the other |  | *She: her coven, her vengeance. He: his faith, his brothers, his identity.* |
| The political crisis point |  | *Her identity is revealed publicly; he must choose who to betray.* |
| How it resolves |  | *They break from both sides, forge something new that honors neither old loyalty.* |

> *Final Thought
>
> Politics Creates Real Stakes
>
> Political worldbuilding in romantasy isn't about creating a realistic government — it's about creating meaningful obstacles that require growth, sacrifice, or systemic change to overcome. If your characters can simply ignore the laws or wait for the problem to disappear, your political obstacles aren't strong enough. But if breaking the law means war, if being caught means death — now you have stakes worth writing about.*

Section 6

# Religion, Myth & Prophecy

Faith, vows, divine purpose, what the gods demand

What do people believe about the divine, the afterlife, destiny, and the meaning of existence? Religion shapes morality, community, identity, and the stories people tell about themselves. In your world, it may also be literally true — gods who answer prayers, prophecies that come to pass, divine magic that proves faith isn't just faith.

This section is divided into two parts:

> #### Part One: The Foundation
>
> What your world believes. The gods (or lack thereof), religious institutions, daily practice, sacred stories, and the afterlife. This is the spiritual reality your characters inhabit regardless of their romantic situation.

> #### Part Two: Religion & Romance
>
> How religious beliefs create romantic tension. Divine law that forbids unions, prophecies that promise or threaten, the crisis of faith when love conflicts with belief.

**You need both.**

- [Common Mistakes](#s6-mistakes)

- [The Afterlife](#s6-afterlife)
- [Mythology & Sacred Stories](#s6-mythology)

### Part Two: Religion & Romance

- [Personal Faith vs. Institutional Religion](#s6-personal-faith)
- [Daily Practice & Devotion](#s6-devotion)
- [Sacred Time & Romantic Opportunity](#s6-sacred-time)
- [Religious Community & Belonging](#s6-community)
- [Religious Law & Doctrine](#s6-law)
- [Crisis of Faith](#s6-crisis)
- [The Priesthood & Religious Authority](#s6-priesthood)
- [Sacred Spaces in Romance](#s6-spaces-romance)
- [Prophecy & Destiny](#s6-prophecy)

- [Chosen Ones & Divine Purpose](#s6-chosen)
- [Religious Difference & Interfaith Love](#s6-interfaith)
- [Sin, Virtue & Religious Guilt](#s6-sin)
- [The Afterlife & Sacrifice](#s6-afterlife-sacrifice)
- [Integration Check](#s6-integration)
- [Quick Reference](#s6-quick-ref)

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question will apply to your story. **Skip what doesn't.**

**If religion is background, keep it simple.** Not every romantasy needs theological complexity.

**If your world has no gods or organized religion, adapt or skip.**

**If you're drawing on real religions, be thoughtful** about what you're borrowing and whether your portrayal is respectful.

# Part One: The Foundation

## The Divine: What Exists

The first question: What higher powers exist in your world, and are they real?

This matters enormously for your story. If gods are confirmed and active, "faith" isn't really faith — it's acknowledgment of fact. Characters can't genuinely doubt when miracles happen regularly. If gods are uncertain or absent, characters can struggle with belief itself, not just obedience.

### Types of Divine Systems

Different theological structures create different story possibilities. A world with many gods allows characters to choose patrons, play deities against each other, or serve different masters. A world with one god creates clearer moral stakes but less room for religious diversity.

**How to write it:** Show faith through habit, not declaration. A devout character touches their holy symbol before danger, whispers a prayer when afraid, uses their god's name in oaths. Their faith shapes how they interpret events — that timely rescue wasn't luck, it was providence. Don't explain the theology; let characters live it. When writing characters of different faiths interacting, let their assumptions clash in small ways: different words for luck, different gestures of respect, different things that feel sacred or profane. Faith becomes visible in what a character won't do, what costs they're willing to pay, and what comforts them when everything else fails.

| Type | How It Works | Story Implications |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Monotheism** | One supreme deity | Clear moral law; heresy feels absolute; questioning the faith means questioning everything |
| **Polytheism** | Multiple gods with different domains | Gods can conflict; characters choose patrons; divine politics mirrors mortal politics |
| **Dualism** | Two opposing forces (good/evil, order/chaos) | Cosmic war; sides must be chosen; gray areas feel transgressive |
| **Animism** | Spirits in nature, ancestors, places | Local sacred sites; honoring the dead; magic tied to specific locations |
| **Confirmed gods** | Deities actively manifest and intervene | Faith isn't faith — it's acknowledgment; divine will is knowable |
| **Absent gods** | Gods existed once but are gone/silent | Seeking lost divinity; old rituals with uncertain power; hope or despair |
| **Ambiguous** | Faith without proof; gods may or may not exist | Room for genuine doubt; faith as choice; religious and secular worldviews can coexist |
| **Atheistic** | No gods; religion is cultural/social | Focus on community and tradition; meaning is human-made |

#### Which describes your world?

*Your choice shapes how faith functions in your story and what religious doubt or devotion means for your characters.*

[Your answer here]

### Are the Gods Real?

This changes everything. In a world where gods visibly answer prayers, atheism is unreasonable. In a world where they don't, devout faith is a choice — admirable or foolish depending on perspective.

#### What's the divine reality of your world?

*Whether gods are real and active affects how your characters relate to faith and what divine intervention might mean for their romance.*

| Status | What This Means for Story |
| --- | --- |
| Gods actively intervene | Miracles happen; divine will is knowable; characters can appeal directly to deity |
| Gods exist but are distant | Real but rarely manifest; faith is still faith; characters must act without divine guidance |
| Gods may or may not exist | Ambiguous; genuine belief and genuine doubt are both reasonable positions |
| Gods are dead/departed | Once real, now gone; faith is memory and hope; old magic fading |
| No gods | Religion is human tradition, not supernatural reality; meaning comes from community, not deity |

#### Which describes your world?

*The answer changes everything about how characters experience faith and what defying religious rules might cost.*

[Your answer here]

#### If gods are real, what can they actually do?

*Can gods grant magic? Curse people? Speak through prophets? Raise the dead? Appear in person?*

[Your answer here]

#### What limits divine power?

*Unlimited gods create story problems — why don't they just fix everything? Consider: Can gods do anything, or are they constrained? By each other? By cosmic law? By their own nature? By mortal free will?*

[Your answer here]

## Major Deities & Powers

### Reference: Deity Domains & Aspects

*Use this list for inspiration when creating gods. Pick domains that create story opportunities.*

| Category | Possible Domains |
| --- | --- |
| **Nature & Elements** | Sun, moon, stars, storms, sea, earth, fire, harvest, seasons, animals, forests, rivers |
| **Life & Death** | Birth, fertility, healing, plague, death, the afterlife, ancestors, rebirth, dreams |
| **Human Affairs** | Love, marriage, family, war, peace, justice, vengeance, merchants, travelers, thieves |
| **Knowledge & Craft** | Wisdom, prophecy, secrets, magic, smithing, weaving, music, poetry, writing, time |
| **Abstract Concepts** | Fate, fortune, chaos, order, truth, lies, beauty, boundaries, thresholds, oaths |
| **Emotions & States** | Joy, grief, rage, fear, madness, ecstasy, courage, despair, hope, desire |

*Tip: Gods with overlapping domains create pantheon politics. A god of war and a god of death might compete for soldiers' prayers.*

Identify the gods or spiritual forces that exist in your world. Focus on those relevant to your story — you don't need a complete pantheon, just the ones who matter.

## Religious Institutions

Gods may exist, but institutions are where religion becomes power. The temple, the church, the priesthood — these are human structures, with human politics, human corruption, and human capacity for both good and harm.

#### How much power does religious authority have?

Religious power can be purely spiritual (moral authority, influence over believers) or extend to political, legal, military, and economic spheres. The more institutional power religion holds, the higher the stakes when characters defy it.

## Sacred Spaces

**How to write it:** A sacred space should feel different from the moment of entry. The quality of silence, the smell of incense or old stone, the way sound behaves differently. Show characters adjusting their behavior unconsciously: lowering voices, slowing steps, becoming physically smaller or more careful. Let the space impose rules without anyone stating them — the devout character knows to genuflect; the outsider watches and follows a beat behind. Sacred spaces in romance create charged moments: the confession spoken where lies feel impossible, the forbidden touch in a place that makes it more transgressive, the sanctuary offered that puts both characters under the god's eye. Use the physical details — candlelight, echoing footsteps, cold stone — to create atmosphere that heightens whatever emotional beat the scene carries.

#### What sacred locations exist?

*Sacred spaces can serve as sanctuary, meeting place, or site of spiritual reckoning for your characters.*

| Type | Examples in Your World | Who Can Enter |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Major temples/churches |  |  |
| Local shrines |  |  |
| Natural sacred sites |  |  |
| Pilgrimage destinations |  |  |
| Forbidden/secret holy places |  |  |

#### What makes a space sacred?

*Divine presence? Historical events? Natural features? Consecration rituals?*

[Your answer here]

# Part Two: Religion & Romance

## How to Think About Religion in Romantasy

Religion creates romantic obstacles through **divine law**, **sacred duty**, and **faith itself**. Unlike political obstacles (which can be changed by changing rulers), religious obstacles feel absolute — it's not just the king who forbids this union, it's God.

This is what makes religious obstacles so powerful for romance. The character isn't just breaking a rule — they're sinning. They're not just disappointing their family — they're risking their soul. They're not just choosing love over duty — they're choosing love over everything they believe about the nature of reality.

> #### Divine Law
>
> Religious doctrine that prohibits or requires:
>
> - Forbidden marriages (across faiths, for clergy, between species)
> - Required marriages (to fulfill prophecy, maintain holy bloodlines)
> - Sexual or romantic prohibitions (celibacy, purity requirements)
> - Consequences that aren't just social but spiritual (damnation, loss of divine favor)

> #### Sacred Duty
>
> Religious obligations that compete with love:
>
> - Vows that bind (celibacy, service, holy mission)
> - Chosen one status that demands sacrifice
> - Priestly duties that prevent personal life
> - Divine purpose that conflicts with personal desire

> #### Faith Itself
>
> The internal spiritual dimension:
>
> - Genuine belief that makes violation feel like sin
> - Crisis of faith when love conflicts with doctrine
> - The loss of spiritual community and identity
> - Finding a faith that includes love, or choosing between them

## Common Patterns: Religion in Romantasy

### Religious Situations That Create Romance Tension

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The forbidden union** | Religious law prohibits their marriage | Sin, damnation stakes, crisis of faith |
| **The vow of celibacy** | One has sworn to the gods not to love | Temptation, breaking vows, questioning calling |
| **The interfaith lovers** | Different religions that oppose each other | Conversion pressure, family opposition, whose god wins |
| **The chosen one** | Divine destiny that love interferes with | Duty vs. desire, sacrifice, questioning prophecy |

### Religious Situations (continued)

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The priest and the profane** | Holy figure loves someone "beneath" their calling | Scandal, fall from grace, redefining holiness |
| **The prophesied union** | Fated to be together by divine decree | Is it real love or just destiny? Agency questions |
| **The divine punishment** | Their love angers the gods | Curses, divine intervention, appeasing deity |

### The Prophecy Problem

Prophecy in romance creates a specific tension: if they're fated to be together, do they have a choice? If they don't have a choice, is it really love?

This tension can drive the entire romantic arc. Characters might resist prophecy precisely because they want their love to be chosen, not destined. Or they might doubt their feelings, wondering if they're real or just prophetic compulsion.

| Approach | How It Works |
| --- | --- |
| Prophecy is misinterpreted | They think it means one thing; it means another |
| Prophecy creates resistance | Knowing they're "fated" makes them fight it |
| Prophecy is conditional | Fate is invitation, not command |
| Prophecy doesn't mean happiness | Fated to meet, not to succeed |
| Multiple valid interpretations | Different readings create different expectations |

## Common Mistakes with Religious Worldbuilding

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #1: The Convenient Religion
>
> **What it looks like:** Religious rules exist to create obstacles, but they're inconsistently applied. The church forbids their marriage, but a friendly priest marries them anyway with no consequences.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** If religious obstacles can be conveniently circumvented, they have no weight.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Make religious consequences real. Finding a way around requires genuine sacrifice or risk. The friendly priest who performs the forbidden marriage faces excommunication.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #2: The Religion Without Believers
>
> **What it looks like:** The world has religious institutions and laws, but no one seems to genuinely believe. Religion is only obstacle, never source of meaning.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** It makes religious obstacles feel arbitrary.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Give characters genuine faith or genuine struggle with faith. Show what religion provides beyond rules — comfort in grief, meaning in suffering, community in isolation.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #3: The Strawman Faith
>
> **What it looks like:** The religion that creates obstacles is obviously corrupt, cruel, or foolish. The only devout characters are villains or fools.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** If the religion is clearly wrong, choosing love over faith is obviously correct. There's no internal conflict needed.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Make the faith sympathetic even when it creates obstacles. Good people believe it for understandable reasons. Tension comes from genuinely conflicting goods.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #4: The Vague Prophecy
>
> **What it looks like:** There's a prophecy about the characters, but it's so vague it could mean anything.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Prophecy only creates tension if it makes specific predictions characters must fulfill, avoid, or wrestle with.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Make prophecies specific enough to create real expectations. "The child of two worlds will unite or destroy" creates tension. "Something important will happen" does not.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> Mistake #5: The Painless Faith Departure
>
> **What it looks like:** A character raised in deep faith abandons it easily once love appears. Their entire belief system dissolves without grief or struggle.
>
> **Why it hurts your romance:** Faith isn't just rules — it's identity, community, and worldview. Losing it should hurt.
>
> How to Fix It
>
> Show the cost of losing faith. The prayers that no longer comfort. The community that turns away. Make the choice hard enough that choosing love anyway means something.

## Personal Faith vs. Institutional Religion

There's a difference between what someone believes in their heart and what institution they belong to. A character might deeply love their gods while hating the corrupt priesthood. Another might follow all the rituals without any real belief.

A character with genuine, deep faith faces a different struggle than one who's just going through the motions. The devout character risks their relationship with the divine if they violate religious law. The cultural believer risks community and identity, but not their soul.

#### What does your protagonist actually believe?

*Not what they're supposed to believe — what do they genuinely feel about the divine? Deep faith? Doubt? Going through motions? Complicated relationship with their gods?*

*Their genuine beliefs may align with or differ from your protagonist's, creating harmony or another layer of conflict.*

*The gap between personal belief and institutional religion creates room for characters to redefine faith rather than abandon it.*

| --- | --- | --- |

*Do they believe in the gods but distrust priests? Follow their own interpretation? This creates the possibility of redefining faith rather than abandoning it.*

*Someone with deep faith faces a different struggle than someone who doesn't really believe but is bound by social religion.*

## Religious Community & Belonging

Religious communities provide belonging, identity, support, and social structure. Leaving or being cast out means losing all of that.

What makes religious community different is how total it often is — the people you celebrate with, who helped when your parent died, where you met your closest friends. Losing that community isn't just losing social connections; it's losing a whole world.

For your characters, the question isn't just "what does their religion forbid?" but "what would they lose if they left?" The answer is rarely just "their faith."

#### What religious community do your characters belong to?

*Community is often what people lose first when they defy religious rules. What support, identity, and belonging would they sacrifice?*

## Crisis of Faith

When love challenges belief, faith itself can break. This is one of the most profound internal conflicts — not just "should I break this rule?" but "is everything I believed a lie?"

A crisis of faith feels like the ground dissolving. For someone who genuinely believed, faith was the structure that made sense of everything — why bad things happen, what makes life meaningful, who you are. When faith cracks, all of that cracks with it.

The crisis often starts with a question that won't go away: *Why would a good god forbid this love?* Sometimes answers satisfy. Sometimes the answers feel hollow, and each hollow answer weakens the structure further.

**How to write it:** Don't make faith loss clean or quick. Show the character cycling through stages: trying harder to believe, getting angry at their god, bargaining for exceptions, grieving what they're losing. Let them attempt the old comforts — prayer, ritual, sacred texts — and show those attempts working less and less, or working differently than before. Write the physical sensations of faith crisis: the hollow feeling where certainty used to be, the vertigo of having no ground, the loneliness of losing something that connected them to community and meaning. If faith is rebuilt, show it as something different than before — scarred, harder-won, more honest. The crisis isn't resolved by finding the right argument; it's resolved by learning to live without certainty, or by finding a different kind of faith entirely.

#### Does the relationship cause either character to question their faith?

*What specifically do they question? The existence of gods? The rightness of doctrine? The authority of religious leaders? The goodness of a god who forbids love?*

[Your answer here]

#### What would it mean for them to lose their faith?

*Not just leaving a religion — losing the belief itself. What would that cost? What would fill the void?*

[Your answer here]

#### Do they try to reconcile their faith with their feelings?

*How? Reinterpreting doctrine? Finding loopholes? Believing the gods would understand? Distinguishing between divine will and institutional corruption?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is there a moment when faith breaks — or is renewed?

*The crisis point when they must choose, or when they find a new understanding:*

[Your answer here]

#### Can they find a version of faith that includes their love?

*Is there a way to believe that doesn't require giving up the relationship? This is the "redefining faith" resolution — harder than leaving, sometimes more satisfying.*

[Your answer here]

## Prophecy & Destiny

Prophecy creates the ultimate tension: Are they fated to be together, or fated to destroy each other? And if fate has decided, does their choice even matter?

**How to write it:** The power of prophecy lies in how characters receive it. Slow down the revelation: the speaker's manner, the quality of their voice, the specific words (prophecies should sound distinct from ordinary speech — rhythmic, archaic, ambiguous). Show the listener's physical reaction as meaning sinks in. After the prophecy, show characters obsessing over wording, looking for loopholes, asking themselves what it *really* means. Prophecy creates dramatic irony — readers may understand implications characters miss, or vice versa. Use asymmetric knowledge: one character knows a prophecy about their relationship that the other doesn't, and must decide whether to reveal it. The prophecy's shadow should fall across their choices: is this feeling real, or am I just fulfilling what was foretold?

#### Do prophecies exist in your world?

*Prophecy adds a layer of fate vs. choice to your romance. Characters may resist destiny or embrace it.*

If yes:

#### How do prophecies work?

*Divine revelation? Oracle visions? Ancient texts? Prophetic dreams? Astrological calculation?*

[Your answer here]

#### Are prophecies always true, sometimes true, or open to interpretation?

*This determines how much weight prophecy carries. Infallible prophecy is destiny. Fallible prophecy is guidance that might be wrong.*

[Your answer here]

#### Is there a prophecy about your characters or their relationship?

*Prophecies about the romance itself create unique tension around whether love is chosen or fated.*

If yes:

#### What does the prophecy say?

*Write the actual prophecy or its key elements:*

[Your answer here]

#### Who knows about this prophecy?

*Everyone? Select few? Only one character? The asymmetry of knowledge creates tension.*

[Your answer here]

#### Does the prophecy suggest they're fated to be together or fated to be apart?

*Either direction creates tension. Fated together: is the love real? Fated apart: can they defy destiny?*

[Your answer here]

*Characters may embrace, resist, deny, or be torn about what prophecy means for them.*

| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

## The Prophecy Problem: Destiny vs. Choice

If prophecy says they're fated, how do you preserve their agency? Romance readers want to believe love is chosen, not predetermined. The best prophesied-love stories find ways to make destiny feel like choice.

#### Which approach are you using?

*How you handle prophecy shapes whether love feels chosen or predetermined. Most satisfying romances preserve agency.*

- ☐ The prophecy is misinterpreted — what they think it means isn't what it means
- ☐ The prophecy creates the problem — knowing they're "fated" makes them resist
- ☐ The prophecy is conditional — fate is invitation, not command; they still have to choose
- ☐ The prophecy doesn't guarantee happiness — fated to meet, not to love, not to succeed
- ☐ Multiple interpretations exist — different readings create different expectations

#### At what point do they choose each other *despite* or *because of* prophecy?

*The moment where prophecy becomes choice, where destiny is claimed rather than passively received:*

[Your answer here]

#### Can prophecy be avoided or changed?

*If yes — how? At what cost? If prophecy can be defied, their choice to fulfill it means something.*

[Your answer here]

## Chosen Ones & Divine Purpose

Being chosen by the gods is a burden as much as a gift. Divine purpose competes with personal desire — how do you have a life when you're supposed to be saving the world?

#### Is either character marked by divine favor, curse, or destiny?

*Being chosen creates obligations that compete with personal desire. What does destiny demand that love cannot give?*

| Aspect | Details | Example (From Blood and Ash) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Which character |  | *Poppy — the Maiden, supposedly chosen by the gods to Ascend and serve* |
| What marks them |  | *Her title, her isolation, the veil she must wear, the "blessing" of her touch* |
| What they're chosen to do |  | *Ascend and become a holy vessel — but the truth is far darker* |

#### How does divine purpose conflict with personal desire?

*Does love distract from destiny? Does destiny require sacrifice of the relationship?*

[Your answer here]

#### What happens if they refuse their divine purpose?

*What are the stakes of saying no to the gods?*

[Your answer here]

## Religious Difference & Interfaith Love

When characters come from different faiths, they must navigate not just their own beliefs but each other's — and often, the mutual hostility of their religious communities.

Interfaith love creates challenges woven into daily life. Whose holidays do you celebrate? Which faith do you raise children in? When someone dies, whose death rituals bring comfort?

#### Do your characters practice different religions?

*Interfaith love creates daily challenges beyond the initial obstacle: whose traditions, whose holidays, whose gods?*

If yes:

| Character | Faith |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### What does each faith teach about the other?

*Mutual hostility between faiths raises the stakes considerably. Mutual respect makes blending easier but may reduce tension.*

| Faith | What It Teaches About the Other |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist's |  |
| Love Interest's |  |

#### What practical conflicts arise from different faiths?

*Different holy days? Conflicting dietary laws? Different marriage requirements? Children — which faith to raise them in?*

[Your answer here]

#### Would one need to convert for them to marry?

*What would conversion mean — practical formality or genuine betrayal of belief?*

[Your answer here]

#### Can they build a shared spiritual life, or will faith always divide them?

*This question shapes the long-term viability of the relationship. Some couples find synthesis; others accept respectful difference.*

[Your answer here]

## Sin, Virtue & Religious Guilt

Religious shame operates differently than social shame — it's not just about what others think, but about what you believe you fundamentally *are*.

There's a crucial difference between "I'm doing something forbidden" and "I *am* something wrong." The first is about actions — you could stop, repent. The second is about identity, and no amount of repentance can change your nature.

Religious guilt follows you into private moments. It can make love itself feel like proof of sinfulness: *If I were good, I wouldn't want this. The fact that I want it shows what I am.*

#### What does your religion teach about desire and love?

*Sacred gift? Dangerous temptation? Test of faith?*

[Your answer here]

#### What romantic or sexual behaviors are considered sinful?

*Understanding what's forbidden helps you write the internal conflict of characters who believe they're doing wrong.*

[Your answer here]

#### Does either character feel they're sinning by being in this relationship?

*Religious shame operates differently than social shame. It follows you into private moments and colors how you see yourself.*

| Aspect | Details |
| --- | --- |
| Which character |  |
| Why they feel sinful |  |
| How they reconcile this |  |

#### Does religious shame affect how they behave in the relationship?

*Withdrawal? Self-punishment? Trying to resist feelings? Guilt after intimacy?*

[Your answer here]

#### What would it take to release religious shame?

*Reinterpretation? Leaving the faith? Divine reassurance? Accepting love isn't sin? Building a new moral framework?*

[Your answer here]

## The Afterlife & Sacrifice

Beliefs about death shape how characters approach mortality and sacrifice. What happens after death determines what dying for love means.

#### Can lovers be reunited after death?

*Or does death separate them? Are only certain unions blessed in the afterlife?*

[Your answer here]

#### If one character is willing to die, what do they believe happens next?

*Their afterlife beliefs shape whether dying for love feels like ending or beginning, loss or reunion.*

[Your answer here]

#### Does belief in the afterlife make sacrifice easier or harder?

*If they believe in reunion after death, does that make dying for love more tempting? Or does uncertainty make sacrifice more costly?*

[Your answer here]

#### Does their relationship affect their afterlife fate?

*Does loving the "wrong" person damn them? Does true love grant salvation?*

[Your answer here]

## Integration Check

| Question | Answer | Example (Priory of the Orange Tree) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| How does religion explain or relate to magic? |  | *The Priory's magic comes from the sacred tree — divine gift. Eastern dragons are holy; Western wyrms are evil* |
| How does religion legitimize or conflict with government? |  | *The Queen's right to rule comes from her holy bloodline; the Priory protects the throne but operates outside its law* |
| How do cultural taboos relate to religious law? |  | *Celibacy is a Priory vow, not state law — but breaking it means losing her place in the sacred order* |
| Could your characters use religion to legitimize their relationship? |  | *If the Nameless One falls and she survives, she could argue her love gave her strength — redefining faith to include it* |
| Or does religion make legitimizing impossible? |  | *The vows are clear: a handmaiden serves the queen, not her own heart. There's no loophole, only the choice to break or keep faith* |

## Quick Reference: Religious Stakes

| Element | Your World | Example (Priory-style) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Primary religious obstacle |  | *She's sworn to a celibate religious order; love means breaking sacred vows* |
| What faith demands |  | *Lifelong virginity, devotion to the Damsel, protection of the queen* |
| What they'd lose by violating religious law |  | *Her sisterhood, her purpose, her identity as a handmaiden — everything she's trained for* |
| The prophecy (if applicable) |  | *The Nameless One will rise; only the faithful can stop it — is love distraction or strength?* |
| Key holy day or sacred time |  | *The vigil before taking final vows — the last moment to choose differently* |
| Religious consequence if they're together |  | *Expulsion from the Priory; seen as corrupted, unreliable, fallen* |
| The divine choice (when they must choose faith or love) |  | *The world needs her as a warrior-priestess; she wants a life with the one she loves* |
| How religion resolves |  | *She redefines faith — love as strength, not weakness; service to the world, not just the order* |

> *Final Thought
>
> Faith Must Feel Real
>
> Even if you're not religious, your characters might be. Their faith should feel genuine — not just an obstacle you created. The best religious worldbuilding makes readers understand why a character would choose faith over love, or why abandoning faith is a true sacrifice. Faith provides comfort, community, identity, and meaning. When characters risk losing that for love, it should hurt. Build a religion that matters enough to fight for, or against, or to redefine entirely.*

Section 7

# History & Lore

The past that haunts the present, inherited conflict

History isn't just backstory — it's the weight your characters carry. The past explains why families hate each other, why borders exist, why certain magic is forbidden, why people fear what they fear. In romantasy, history creates obstacles that feel inevitable ("we've been enemies for centuries") and makes overcoming them meaningful ("we're ending an ancient war").

This section is divided into two parts:

> #### Part One: The Foundation
>
> What actually happened in your world's past. The major events, the conflicts, the figures everyone remembers. This is the historical reality that shapes your world regardless of your characters' romantic situation.

> #### Part Two: History & Romance
>
> How the past creates romantic tension. Inherited feuds, ancestral guilt, legendary lovers your characters are compared to, the weight of being told "this has happened before, and it ended badly."

**You need both.**

## A Note Before You Begin

Not every question here will matter for your story. History can stay vague — sometimes "the old war" is enough. Focus on what creates present tension: the question isn't "what happened?" but "what happened that makes their relationship impossible?" Skip sections that don't apply.

# Part One: The Foundation

## How History Works in Your World

Before you can use history to create romantic tension, you need to understand how your world relates to its past. How is history preserved? Who controls what's remembered? How do ordinary people learn about what came before?

### How History Is Recorded

Different methods of recording history shape what survives and how it's understood.

#### What methods preserve history in your world?

| Method | What It Preserves Well | What It Loses | Who Has Access |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| **Oral tradition** | Stories, songs, values | Precise details, dates | Everyone in the culture |
| **Written records** | Facts, dates, official accounts | Perspective of the illiterate | The literate (often elite) |
| **Magical memory** | Perfect recall, emotional truth | May be rare or restricted | Mages, special bloodlines |
| **Living witnesses** | Firsthand accounts | Dies with them (unless immortal) | Those who meet them |
| **Physical artifacts** | Objects, buildings, art | Context, meaning | Those who find/possess them |

#### History Recording Methods (continued)

| Method | What It Preserves Well | What It Loses | Who Has Access |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| **Religious texts** | Sacred events, moral lessons | Often mythologized | Believers, clergy |
| **Bardic/artistic tradition** | Emotional truth, cultural values | May prioritize story over fact | Audiences of performances |

#### Which methods exist in your world?

*The methods shape what kinds of history survive and who has access to it.*

[Your answer here]

#### Which method is considered most authoritative?

*What counts as "real" history vs. "just stories"?*

[Your answer here]

### Who Keeps History

#### Who are the historians and record-keepers?

*Whoever controls the records controls the narrative. Their biases become your world's "official history."*

| Type | What They Preserve | Their Biases |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Court historians** | Royal/noble deeds, official narratives | Favor those in power |
| **Religious archivists** | Sacred history, moral exemplars | Religious interpretation |
| **Academic scholars** | Researched, analytical history | May be detached from lived experience |
| **Bards and storytellers** | Popular history, heroic tales | Favor good stories over accuracy |
| **Family keepers** | Lineage, family deeds | Favor their own bloodline |

#### Who keeps history in your world?

*Focus on the keepers most relevant to your story. Whose version of history do your characters know?*

[Your answer here]

#### What institutions preserve the past?

*Institutions that hold history can become plot elements: libraries to break into, forbidden archives, restricted knowledge.*

| Institution | What It Holds | Who Can Access It |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Libraries/archives | Chronicles of the realm, treaties, lineage records going back twelve generations | Scholars with royal writ; nobility by right; others by petition and bribery |
| Magical repositories | Spell grimoires, accounts of magical catastrophes, records of forbidden workings | Only initiated mages; the knowledge inside is considered too dangerous for the uninitiated |
| Libraries/archives |  |  |
| Temples/religious centers |  |  |
| Royal/noble collections |  |  |
| Bardic colleges |  |  |
| Magical repositories |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

### How Ordinary People Learn History

Elite record-keeping is one thing, but what do common people actually know about the past?

#### How do most people learn about history?

*This shapes what your characters "know" about the past. Oral tradition produces different knowledge than formal archives.*

- Stories from elders and family
- Religious teachings
- Songs, ballads, performances
- Festivals commemorating events
- Monuments and public art
- Formal education (if available)
- Gossip and folk memory

#### Which are most common in your world?

*Consider how your characters learned the history they know. Songs? Grandparents' stories? Temple teachings?*

[Your answer here]

#### What history do children learn?

*What stories are they told? What do they grow up "knowing" about the past?*

[Your answer here]

#### Is there a gap between elite and common historical knowledge?

*Do scholars know different things than farmers? Do different classes have different histories?*

[Your answer here]

## What Survives and What's Lost

Not everything from the past makes it to the present. Understanding what's preserved and what's lost shapes your world.

#### What survives well in your world?

*Stone buildings? Written records? Magical artifacts? Oral traditions? Bloodline memories?*

## How People Relate to the Past

Different cultures have different relationships with their history — some venerate it, some try to escape it, some barely think about it.

#### What's your culture's attitude toward the past?

*This shapes how your characters relate to tradition, change, and the weight of ancestral expectations.*

| Attitude | How It Manifests |
| --- | --- |
| **Reverent** | Ancestors honored, traditions preserved, past seen as golden |
| **Pragmatic** | Past is useful for lessons, not sacred |
| **Forward-looking** | Past is burden to escape, future matters more |
| **Cyclical** | Past repeats, history is pattern to understand |
| **Traumatized** | Past is wound, avoided or obsessed over |

#### Which describes your world (or different groups within it)?

*Different groups may have different attitudes. Characters from different backgrounds may clash over what the past means.*

[Your answer here]

#### How do people interact with historical sites?

*Are ruins sacred? Dangerous? Tourist attractions? Forbidden? Looted for materials?*

[Your answer here]

#### Do people venerate ancestors?

*How? Shrines? Prayers? Consulting spirits? Obligation to fulfill their wishes?*

[Your answer here]

#### Are there historical commemorations?

*Holidays marking past events? Monuments? Annual remembrances?*

[Your answer here]

## Building Your World's Timeline

Now that you understand how history works, identify the events that shaped your world. You don't need a complete timeline — focus on what matters for your story.

### Layers of History

Think of history in layers, each more distant and more mythologized:

| Layer | Time Frame | How It's Known | How It Feels |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
| **Living memory** | Last 50-80 years | People remember; grandparents tell stories | Raw, personal, contested |
| **Recent history** | 80-200 years | Written records; family stories | Established but still debated |
| **Distant history** | 200-500 years | Official histories; may be simplified | Feels settled, "the way things are" |
| **Ancient history** | 500+ years | Myth mixed with fact; legendary | Foundational, almost sacred |
| **Mythic past** | Time before time | Purely legendary; may involve gods | Explains origins, sets cosmic rules |

#### Which layers matter for your story?

*Focus on the eras that directly affect your characters' lives and relationships.*

[Your answer here]

## The Shape of History

#### What's the overall arc of your world's history?

*The shape of history affects the mood of your world. Decline feels different from recovery or cycles.*

| Arc Type | Pattern | Example |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **Decline** | Golden age fell; things getting worse | Lost magic, fallen empire, diminishing bloodlines |
| **Recovery** | Catastrophe happened; rebuilding | Post-war reconstruction, recovering from plague |
| **Cycle** | Patterns repeat; history rhymes | Regular conflicts, recurring prophecies |
| **Progress** | Things improving over time | Rising civilization, expanding knowledge |
| **Stasis** | Little fundamental change | Ancient traditions maintained, rigid social order |
| **Conflict** | Ongoing struggle defines history | Eternal war, competing powers |

#### Which describes your world?

*You can combine patterns or note that different groups perceive different arcs.*

[Your answer here]

## Major Historical Events

Identify the events that shaped your world. Focus on those relevant to your story.

**How to write it:** History enters your story through fragments: a phrase everyone knows, a monument passed without explanation, an old song with verses no one remembers the meaning of. Let history emerge through what characters take for granted — "the wall has always been there" — rather than through exposition. When characters do discuss history, give them opinions: one calls it "the Great Liberation," another calls it "the Betrayal." Let historical memory create tension between characters who were taught different versions of the same events. The most powerful historical moments are when a character discovers that what they were taught isn't true, or when they stand in a place where history happened and feel its weight.

### The Defining Event

#### What single historical event most shaped the current world?

*This is the event everyone references, the "before and after" that defines eras. How does it affect your characters?*


#### The Defining Event

| Aspect | Details | Example (ACOTAR) |
| --- | --- | --- |
| What happened |  | *The War — fae vs. humans, ending with the creation of the Wall and the Treaty* |
| When |  | *500 years ago — ancient enough to be legend, recent enough that fae remember it* |
| Who was involved |  | *All seven High Lords united (rare), human armies, great losses on both sides* |
| Immediate consequences |  | *The Wall created, fae and humans separated, the Treaty binding both sides* |
| Long-term effects still felt today |  | *Fae see humans as lesser; humans fear fae; the Wall shapes all politics; no one trusts the peace to last* |

### Other Significant Events

#### List 2-4 other events that matter for your story:

| Event | When | What Happened | Current Relevance |
| --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  |  |  |  |
|  |  |  |  |
|  |  |  |  |

## Historical Conflicts

### Reference: What Causes Wars & Conflicts

*Use this list when you need a believable reason for historical (or current) conflict in your world.*

| Category | Conflict Triggers |
| --- | --- |
| **Resources** | Land, water, trade routes, magical resources, fertile territory, mining rights, access to the sea |
| **Power & Succession** | Disputed throne, rival claimants, power vacuum, assassination, broken alliance, betrayed treaty |
| **Religion & Ideology** | Heresy, forced conversion, holy sites, competing gods, prophecy fulfillment, religious persecution |
| **Identity & Culture** | Ethnic cleansing, cultural suppression, forbidden magic, species conflict, language bans, slavery |
| **Honor & Vengeance** | Blood feud, broken oath, unavenged murder, stolen bride, insulted ruler, desecrated sacred site |
| **Expansion & Fear** | Imperial ambition, preemptive strike, border incursion, refugee crisis, rising power threatens neighbors |
| **Magical Causes** | Magical plague, curse, prophecy, magical weapon discovered, ley line shift, forbidden magic unleashed |

*Tip: The best conflicts have multiple causes. A war might start over resources but be remembered as religious.*

Wars and conflicts create lasting divisions. Even centuries later, people remember who fought whom.

#### What major conflicts shaped your world?

*Focus on conflicts relevant to your characters. Old wars create old resentments that live on in families and nations.*

| Conflict | Who vs. Who | Why | Who Won | Lasting Effects |
| --- | --- | --- | --- | --- |
|  |  |  |  |  |
|  |  |  |  |  |

#### Are any historical conflicts still unresolved?

*Cold tensions, simmering resentments, truces that aren't really peace?*

[Your answer here]

## Who Controls the Narrative

History isn't neutral. Someone decides what's remembered and what's forgotten.

#### Who writes the official history?

*Rulers? Religious authorities? Scholars? The victors?*

[Your answer here]

#### What perspectives are missing from official history?

*The conquered? The poor? Certain species? Magic users?*

[Your answer here]

#### Are there competing versions of history?

*Different peoples telling different stories about the same events?*

[Your answer here]

## Historical Figures

Every history has its heroes, villains, and legendary names.

#### Who are the great heroes everyone knows?

*Heroes create expectations. Is your protagonist compared to one? Does the love interest idolize someone your protagonist's people consider a villain?*

| Hero | What They Did | How They're Remembered |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Eiravel the Undying | United the warring kingdoms; died holding the mountain pass against the demon horde alone | Statues in every city square; invoked before battles; the standard all rulers are measured against |
| Lady Morgaine Blackthorn | Defied the High Council to save her lover from execution; sparked the Rebellion of Roses | Controversial: traitor to some, symbol of love's power to others; her ballad still sung at weddings |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

#### Who are the great villains?

*Villains create cautionary tales. Is either character compared to a villain? What would it mean to be descended from one?*

| Villain | What They Did | How They're Remembered |
| --- | --- | --- |
| The Pale King | Broke the ancient compact; unleashed the Blight that killed half the continent | His name is never spoken aloud; his sealed tower still stands, forbidden |
| Queen Seraphine the Betrayer | Poisoned the peace delegation; murdered her husband to seize sole power | Her portrait hangs reversed in the palace; her descendants changed their names |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

#### Are any of these figures relevant to your characters?

*Ancestors? Role models? Cautionary examples?*

## Lost and Forbidden Knowledge

#### What was lost to history?

*Forgotten magic? Destroyed civilizations? Suppressed truths?*

[Your answer here]

#### Why was it lost?

*Deliberately destroyed? Simply forgotten? Hidden for protection?*

[Your answer here]

#### Does this lost knowledge matter for your story?

*Lost knowledge can drive plot: quests for artifacts, discoveries that change everything, forbidden knowledge that explains current problems.*

# Part Two: History & Romance

## How to Think About History in Romantasy

History creates romantic obstacles through **inheritance** (family feuds, ancestral crimes, old alliances), **precedent** (legendary lovers, previous forbidden romances, prophecies), and **weight** (breaking from tradition, ending ancient feuds, choosing love over ancestral loyalty). Your characters didn't choose the past, but they're bound by it.

## Common Patterns: History in Romantasy

### Historical Situations That Create Romance Tension

| Pattern | How It Works | What It Creates |
| --- | --- | --- |
| **The feud** | Their families/peoples have been enemies for generations | "We're supposed to hate each other" |
| **The ancestral crime** | One's ancestor wronged the other's | Guilt, resentment, demand for justice |
| **The legendary parallel** | A famous couple mirrors their situation | "You know how that ended" |
| **The discovered truth** | Hidden history changes everything | "Everything we believed was wrong" |
| **The cyclical doom** | This pattern always ends badly | "We're different" vs. "History repeats" |
| **The long-lived lover** | One has centuries of history the other doesn't | Asymmetric experience, past loves |

### The "Legendary Lovers" Dynamic

Many romantasies invoke famous couples from their world's history — doomed lovers, triumphant pairs, cautionary tales. This creates specific tensions:

| How It's Used | Effect |
| --- | --- |
| **Warning** | "Don't repeat their mistake" |
| **Pressure** | "Live up to their example" |
| **Doom** | "You'll end the same way" |
| **Hope** | "If they made it, so can we" |
| **Irony** | "The legend got the story wrong" |

## Common Mistakes with Historical Worldbuilding

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> #### Mistake #1: The Irrelevant History
>
> Extensive historical detail that never affects the actual story. History should create pressure on the present, not just exist as decoration. For every historical detail, ask: "How does this affect my characters right now?" If it doesn't, cut it.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> #### Mistake #2: The Convenient Discovery
>
> Characters discover a hidden historical truth that perfectly solves their problem. If "our peoples have been enemies for centuries" is resolved by "actually the whole feud was a misunderstanding," the resolution is too easy. Historical discoveries should complicate as much as they clarify.

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> #### Mistake #3: The Vague Ancient Grudge
>
> Characters are from feuding families, but the feud is so ancient and vague it has no emotional weight. Abstract ancient hatred doesn't create real tension. Make it specific and recent enough to hurt: not "ancient grudge" but "your grandfather burned my grandmother's village."

> **Common Mistake:**
>
> #### Mistake #4: The Static Past
>
> History is just facts to be recited, not something characters have different perspectives on. Let characters disagree about history. Let the past be as contested as the present.

## Who Tells the Story

When characters come from different backgrounds, they often know different versions of the same history. This creates conflict that isn't about facts but about perspective.

#### If your characters come from different backgrounds, do they know different histories?

*One's heroes might be the other's villains. One's glorious victory might be the other's massacre. These aren't lies — they're different truths.*

| Event | Protagonist's Version | Love Interest's Version |
| --- | --- | --- |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

#### What happens when they compare histories?

*Do they fight about it? Try to understand? Discover they were both taught incomplete versions?*

[Your answer here]

#### What would it mean if the "other side's" history turned out to be true?

*This is a powerful moment: discovering your people's heroes were villains, or that the enemy's grievances are justified.*

[Your answer here]

## The Recent Past: Living Memory

History that people alive can remember feels most immediate. This is where wounds are still fresh and stories are still contested.

*Do their families still hate each other? Is one family blamed by the other?*

[Your answer here]

## Family Stories & Personal History

Beyond the big events, every family has its own stories — the tales told around the fire, the legends of ancestors, the grudges passed down. Family stories shape identity. They tell you who your people are, what you should value, who you should hate or honor. When two people fall in love, their family stories either align or clash.

**How to write it:** Let family stories surface naturally — triggered by objects, places, or moments. A character picks up their grandmother's ring and remembers the story of how she survived the siege. They pass an old oak and mention that's where their father proposed. Don't tell the whole story at once; let details accumulate. The power is in how the story has been shaped by retelling: what's emphasized, what's softened, what's left out. When characters share family stories with each other, it's an act of intimacy — they're saying "this is where I come from, this is who my people are." When family stories clash ("your great-grandfather killed my ancestor"), they create conflicts that feel both ancient and personal.

#### What stories does your protagonist's family tell about themselves?

*The founding ancestor? The great hero? The terrible loss? The family shame?*

[Your answer here]

#### What stories does your love interest's family tell?

*Their family stories may complement, contradict, or directly oppose your protagonist's family mythology.*

[Your answer here]

#### How do these family stories shape who each character believes they should be?

*We're shaped by the stories we inherit. What expectations do family legends place on your characters?*

| Character | What Family Stories Taught Them to Be |
| --- | --- |
| Protagonist |  |
| Love Interest |  |

#### Are there family stories that contradict each other?

*Does one family's hero appear as a villain in the other's stories?*

[Your answer here]

#### What family story has each character never questioned — until now?

*Falling in love with the "wrong" person often forces us to question the stories we were raised on.*

[Your answer here]

| How history resolves |  |

> ### Final Thought: History Gives Weight to Choices
>
> Without history, your characters' choices happen in a vacuum. With history, every choice either continues a pattern or breaks one. "We've been enemies for five hundred years" makes reconciliation meaningful. "My ancestor killed yours" makes forgiveness costly. Build a history that makes the present feel inevitable — then let your characters prove the future doesn't have to be.

# Section 8

# Language & Communication

Language is intimacy and barrier in equal measure. Shared language creates understanding; language barriers force vulnerability and trust. In romantasy, language does more than convey information — it reveals power, creates secrets, enables private communication, and forces characters to truly listen to each other.

> ### Language does three crucial things in romantasy:
>
> 1. **Creates barriers that require trust** — When characters don't share a language, communication becomes an act of vulnerability
> 2. **Enables secret communication** — Private languages, coded messages, magical communication others can't intercept
> 3. **Reveals power and status** — Who speaks the "proper" language, who controls literacy, who has access to knowledge

The key is understanding: What language barriers exist between your characters? What private communication methods do they develop? What does learning each other's language mean for intimacy?

Don't build elaborate linguistic systems for their own sake. Build language that serves your story: misunderstandings that lead to clarity, private words only they understand, the vulnerability of teaching and learning.

## Soft vs Hard Magic Systems

Your magic doesn't have to fit neatly into one category—most systems fall somewhere in between. But understanding this spectrum helps you use magic well in your story.

### The Spectrum

**Hard magic** has clear rules that readers can learn. When your character solves a problem with magic, it feels earned because the reader saw it coming. **Soft magic** stays mysterious. It creates wonder and atmosphere rather than puzzles to solve.

The key question: How much do your readers need to understand for your plot to work?

| Aspect | Hard Magic | Soft Magic |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Rules | Clearly defined, consistent | Vague, mysterious |
| Limitations | Built into the system | Often external (cost, taboo) |
| Problem-solving | Strategic use of rules | Symbolic, emotional |
| Examples | Avatar, Mistborn | LOTR, fairy tales |

*Most stories blend both approaches. Your protagonist might use a hard magic system while encountering soft, mysterious magic from ancient sources or other cultures. The key is consistency: if your magic solves problems, readers need to understand its rules first.*


## Types of Magic Systems

You don't have to reinvent magic from scratch. These common archetypes give you a starting point.

Feel free to combine them—most interesting systems do.

- **Elemental** — Magic tied to natural forces (fire, water, earth, air) or their combinations
- **Hereditary/Bloodline** — Magic passed genetically through family lines; often tied to ancestry or species
- **Incantation-based** — Power accessed through spoken spells, words of power, or specific phrases
- **Ritual magic** — Magic requiring ceremonies, specific timing, sacrifices, or group participation
- **Tool-based** — Magic channeled through wands, staffs, artifacts, or enchanted objects
- **Divine/Gifted** — Power granted by gods, spirits, or higher beings; may require faith or service
- **Innate/Wild** — Magic one is born with, often uncontrolled or instinctive; may require training to master
- **Learned/Academic** — Magic studied like a science or craft; requires education and practice
- **Sympathetic/Contagion** — Magic where like affects like, or contact creates connection (voodoo dolls, true names)
- **Soul/Life-force based** — Magic that costs vitality, lifespan, or spiritual energy to cast

*Consider which types exist in your world, who has access to each, and what combining types might mean for your characters.*


## Your Character's Spells & Powers

If your characters use specific spells, abilities, or powers, it helps to track them. This keeps your magic consistent and reminds you what tools your protagonist has available.

You don't need to fill every row. Start with the abilities that matter most to your story. Add more as they develop.

#### Protagonist's Magical Abilities

*Track your protagonist's powers, how they work, and what they cost. This prevents inconsistencies and helps you plan dramatic moments.*

| Ability/Spell | What It Does | Cost/Limitation |
| --- | --- | --- |
| Shadow-step | Move instantly between shadows within sight | Exhausting; can't carry others; fails in full sunlight |
| Pain-reading | Sense others' physical injuries through touch | Feels echoes of their pain; overwhelming in crowds |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

*Knowing both characters' abilities helps you plan how their powers might complement, clash, or create tension.*

| --- | --- | --- |

|  |  |  |
|  |  |  |

|  |  |  |

*Powers that will emerge later in the story. Planning these helps you foreshadow them earlier.*

| The shift in address (from → to) |  |
| The true name revelation (if applicable) |  |

> ### Final Thought: Language Is Connection
>
> Language barriers force characters to find other ways to connect — through action, through patience, through the vulnerability of misunderstanding. But language is also what's not said. The silences. The lies. The truths too heavy to speak. The way someone's voice sounds when they say your name.
>
> The act of learning someone's language is an act of investment. It says: "Your world matters enough for me to learn how you see it." Build language that creates both barriers and bridges. Make your characters work to understand each other — and make that work meaningful.
>
> Every word they teach each other, every silence they share, every shift from formal to intimate should mark a deepening connection. Don't just have them speak the same language. Make them learn each other.

# Section 9

# Technology & Infrastructure

Technology level determines what's possible and what's difficult. Can people communicate instantly across distances, or do letters take weeks? Can they travel in hours, or does separation mean months apart? Does infrastructure exist to support large populations, or is survival a daily struggle?

> ### Technology does three crucial things in romantasy:
>
> 1. **Sets the pace of life** — How much time do people spend on survival vs. leisure? Who has time for anything beyond work?
> 2. **Determines connection and separation** — Travel speed and communication methods define what distance means
> 3. **Creates or removes obstacles** — Technology can solve problems (or its absence creates them)

The key is understanding what your tech level makes easy and what it makes hard. Don't get lost in technical details. Focus on how technology shapes daily life, what it enables, and what it prevents.
